M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
Acknowledgements 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         I 
                 
 
Acknowledgements 
And yet, for the second time has arrived the time to deliver a thesis on 
Light Rail; after my Bachelor thesis that was about an aspect of LR infrastructure.  
I would first of all thank my supervisors, Alex Landex and Steven Harrods. 
They both have been extremely helpful in addressing me, with my potentially 
endless questions, towards the end of a hard – yet inspiring and entertaining – 
academic path. Alex, with his huge knowledge and clear passion for railways, has 
been a unique and precious daily compass. Despite that during the semester he 
became first husband and then father, Alex has always found time to guide me in 
me work. Also Steven gave me a big help and key suggestions. I particularly 
thank him for accepting, when I was in the USA for an exchange semester, to 
become my supervisor without having met me; I not am sure everyone would 
have done that. 
Next, I have to say a big thanks to the companies, Rambøll and 
Metroselskabet & Hovedstadens Letbane, that sponsored my work and provided 
my with every kind of support I needed. I am grateful to plenty of people in both 
companies, for the help and care they have been showing throughout the entire 
semester. However, I want to especially thank Fosca Romani for her informal but 
extremely beneficial supervising activities as well as Peter Årkrog and Line 
Matthai for making my semester in running as smooth as possible. 
The list of family members and friends I am grateful to would not fit this 
page, for everyone contributed, in some ways, in forming my personality and in 
achieving my goals. I do my best to thank them with the little everyday thinks, as 
the smallest act of kindness is worth more than the grandest intention.

M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
ABSTRACT 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         1 
                 
 
ABSTRACT 
Light Rail are a spectacular exception in the field of transport, where 
antiquate technologies rarely make a comeback. Despite being labelled as slow 
and obstructing after World War Second, LR turn out to be one of the leading 
urban transport means in the last decade. The numbers of this phenomenon are 
astonishing. In all four biggest Danish cities, a LR network is under construction 
or design. The city of Florence, Italy, with less than 400.000 inhabitants, will 
enjoy six LR lines by 2025. Hence, the need of study LR and its related equipment 
is crucial in fuelling their sustainable growth and meeting the people expectation 
for a comfortable, reliable, and environmental friendly means of urban transport.  
Within the area of LR, signalling systems are vital in ensuring safe and 
optimized transport operations. This thesis attempts to systematically studying 
the LR signalling system and its components as well as to outline where such a 
system fits in the greater areas of railway and Light Rail. Besides, assessing how 
signalling-related parameters affect operation and, thus, giving valuable project 
guidelines are also goal of the thesis. This is done, among other things, by 
comparing data from existing LR lines and interpreting the results from the 
analysis. Finally, the knowledge, theories, and analysis presented in the thesis 
are applied in describing and assessing the expected LR signalling performances 
of the Copenhagen Light Rail on Ring 3 project, expected to start operations in 
2021. 
Although the existing literature and academic papers cover, separately, 
some of the presented topics, there seems to be a lack of all-embracing sources. 
Therefore, a holistic approach with focus on LR signalling in its entirety has been 
followed throughout the whole thesis work. Thus, it has been chosen to present a 
broader spectrum of LR signalling aspects and their interconnections; rather 
than focussing on a deep study of limited portions of the subject. This was also 
the motivations of the institution and companies supporting my work, from 
which they expect to benefit in carrying out they study and project activities. 
Chapter 1 gives a very general introduction of railways, signalling, and LR. 
The aim is to describe the basic functionalities and working principles of such 
topics that are going to be used in following parts of the thesis. Besides, an 
historical evolution of LR is presented. Learning objective 1 is largely covered 
within this chapter. 
Chapter 2 present a more detailed explanation of LR signalling and its 
design. The issues related with the LR running modes and LR-road Intersection 
==PAGE:25==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
ABSTRACT 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         2 
                 
 
Priority Management, as well as their historical evolution, are explained. Besides, 
the actual need for LR signalling, its requirements, and working philosophy, are 
presented. Parts of the learning objectives 1 and 2 are covered within this 
chapter. 
Chapter 3 provides a categorization of the equipment making up the LR 
signalling system. Their working principles and various functional architectures 
are assessed; together with analyses identigying pros and cons of different LR 
signalling equipment alternatives. Part of learning objective 2 and 3 are covered 
within this chapter. 
Chapter 4 features a more detailed outline of LR signalling application 
contexts. Here, it is examined how the equipment presented in Chapter 4 
actualizes the driving regimes and operational philosophies behind LR signalling 
presented in Chapter 2. Part of learning objective 2 and 3 are covered within this 
chapter. 
Chapter 5 deals with the issues related with LR-road intersections and 
Intersection Priority Management System. A thoroughly assessment of LR-road 
intersections is presented. Next, an explanation of various LR IPMS working 
principles and functional architectures follows, together with an assessment of 
their pro and cons. Learning objectives 4 and 5 are covered within this chapter. 
In Chapter 6 the benchmarking of various LR line data, in terms of 
signalling and IPMS, is carried out. The worldwide geographical distribution of 
LR networks is illustrated in order to assess the quantitative importance of 
various countries in terms of LR. Next, a number of analysis and comparison are 
carried out with the aim of judge and interpret which of the signalling-related 
factors, explained in the previous chapters, have impact on LR performances. 
Learning objective 6 is covered within this chapter. 
Finally, in Chapter 7 an analysis, in term of LR signalling, of the 
Copenhagen LR on Ring 3 project is carried out. A project description is 
presented, along with an analysis of its peculiarities. Next, the theories and 
knowledge shown in the previous chapters, together with the results from the 
benchmarking analyses, are applyed to assess the designed LR line performances 
and to formulate possible improvements.  Learning objective 7 is covered within 
this chapter. 
Learning objectives 8 and 9, as well as the general DTU learning 
objectives, have always been a vital compass thought the entire thesis work. 
The present thesis concludes a two year DTU Master Programme in 
Transport and Logistics. The precious knowledge gathered during the Master, 
both in terms of Transport in general and more specifically in Railway

Engineering, has been essential in developing the present document.

M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
RAILWAYS, SIGNALLING, AND LIGHT RAIL: GENERAL OVERVIEW 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         4 
                 
 
1 RAILWAYS, SIGNALLING, AND LIGHT RAIL: 
GENERAL OVERVIEW 
1.1 Introduction to Railway 
According to the Oxford Dictionary of English, railways are defined as “a 
track made of steel rails along which trains run” or also a “a network of tracks with 
the trains, organization, and personnel required for its working” (The Oxford 
Dictionary of English). Railways are a mean of mechanized land transport 
suitable both for passenger movement and freight haulage. Nowadays, railways 
represent the backbone of the transportation system in many countries and they 
positively contribute to social and economic growth all over the World.  
Even though there are some historical uncertainties, it is commonly 
agreed that the first railway line was built around 300 years ago in the United 
Kingdom. Its main purpose was to provide a durable, smooth, and solid guide 
way for passenger and freight carriages, which could run with little tractive 
effort due to low friction. In fact, at that time, the quality and reliability of land 
transports was very poor. They were negatively influenced by non-paved ways 
subject to mud, uneven running surfaces, and lack of bridges. This resulted in 
long, expensive, and dangerous travels (Morgan 1971). 
 
Figure 1-1: An old picture where a horse-powered carriage had problems to move due to muddy 
surfaces. Next to it, a railway line under construction. Source: http://torontoist.com/wp-
content/uploads/2014/03/e-2014_03_08_f1244_it0039_640.jpg [6 March 2015] 
The technical evolution of railways was initially focussed on making the 
infrastructure more efficient, following trial and error processes. Ever since, the 
==PAGE:28==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
RAILWAYS, SIGNALLING, AND LIGHT RAIL: GENERAL OVERVIEW 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         5 
                 
 
goal of railway engineers has been to adapt the railway infrastructure and its 
equipment to accommodate increasingly heavy loads and faster locomotives 
(Kerr 2003). Their incessant work brought modern high-speed trains, able to 
safely reach and exceed 550 km/h, as well as efficient and sustainable rail-based 
transport means, both for urban passenger transport and for long distance 
freight hauling.  
1.1.1 Advantages and Disadvantages 
The unique peculiarity of railways as land transport is that the vehicles 
have steel wheels and run on a fixed steel guide ways. Resulting in the following 
main advantages (Hay 1982): 
 
High motion efficiency from the mechanical, environmental, and 
economic standpoint, due to very low rolling friction between steel 
wheels and steel rails. The coefficient of friction between dry steel wheels 
and rai steel is circa 0,4, whereas the one between rubber and asphalt is 
about 10 times higher 
 
A very optimized infrastructure’s material utilization ratio, thanks to the 
fixed guide ways that allows both less material wastage and an enhanced 
exploitation of the material’s mechanical properties. Conversely, 
highways are realized with more material wastage and lower utilization 
ratio; due to the unpredictability of the vehicle’s weight application point, 
since road vehicles do not have fixed guide ways 
 
The ability of carrying large amount of people and goods in relatively 
small spaces 
 
The possibility of different coaches coupling, giving the operators high 
flexibility  
 
Very high transport safety thanks to advanced managerial and 
operational practices as well as sophisticated signalling systems 
 
High riding comfort, due to a very smooth running surface and smartly 
engineered curve design 
 
The potential, with existing technologies, for electrified railways of being 
alimented with 100% renewable energy and produce zero carbon 
emissions.  
On the other hand, the main disadvantages are (Hay 1982): 
 The construction of long railway line requires large land usage and 
resources compared with water and air form of transport 
 Fixed guide ways have low flexibility in terms of infrastructure usage, as 
==PAGE:29==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
RAILWAYS, SIGNALLING, AND LIGHT RAIL: GENERAL OVERVIEW 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         6 
                 
 
the route taken by the train is set by the interlinked mechanical guidance 
system of flanged wheels and rail, and it can be changed only at turnouts 
 On single-track lines, trains coming from opposite directions can pass 
each other only at some predetermined locations, such as loops and 
stations 
 Faster trains that can overtake slower ones in predetermined locations 
only, with the slow train sometimes required to completely stop to be 
overtaken 
 As the rolling friction is small, trains generally have poor retarding 
response and, accordingly, longer braking distances than road vehicles. 
1.2 Introduction to Railway Signalling 
Rail signalling systems are needed to ensure first safe and, next, 
optimized train traffic operations. Overall, the main goals of signalling systems 
can be summarized in: first, ensuring safe train operations; secondly, regulation 
and optimization of traffic considering various factors. In those regards, 
information transmission and processing technologies have always played a 
crucial role in the field of rail signalling (Thegg & Vlasenko 2009). 
Signalling systems play a critical and vital role in the railway field due to 
its unique peculiarities of being a system where vehicles with steel wheels run 
on fixed steel guide ways. As a result, train cannot simply overtake and pass each 
other. More importantly, braking distance at as little as 50 to 70 km/h 
(depending on local conditions and retarding technologies) is often longer than 
the train driver’s sight distance. Therefore, the safety has to be supplemented by 
other precautions to indicate the driver whether the track ahead is clear, and if 
the train has to stop or reduce speed before reaching a potential danger. 
Generally speaking, the signalling system is primarily designed for 
regulating two kinds of train movements: in the same direction (block sections) 
and crossings (terminuses, turnouts, single track, etc.). The solution to those 
technical problems has been solved with the implementation of sophisticated 
signalling solutions that has been evolving and adapting overtime. Lately, state-
of-the-art signalling technologies allow, among other things, driverless train 
operations, heavy haul freight transport, safe high-speed runs, and optimal 
urban transit solutions (Thegg & Vlasenko 2009). 
Even though worldwide there are differences in the ways the signalling 
systems are designed, reflecting diverse operational philosophies; the basic 
underlying principles are very similar. That is, signalling systems are strictly 
needed to guarantee and enhance safety in those circumstances where 
==PAGE:30==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
RAILWAYS, SIGNALLING, AND LIGHT RAIL: GENERAL OVERVIEW 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         7 
                 
 
technically unmonitored human operations may lead to dangerous failures and 
accidents. In particular, interlocking equipment where developed to minimize or 
eliminate potential dispatcher mistakes, whereas automatic train protection 
systems have the function to protect against possible train drivers’ errors. 
Conversely, in the case that train operations are carried out with acceptable 
safety levels without signalling equipment, it is common practice to not install 
them to save resources. For instance, in many situations LR operations do not 
necessarily need signalling equipment for safety reasons, as will be thoroughly 
explained in chapter 2. Besides, as in a multitude of different technical systems, 
considerations about reliability, availability, maintainability, and safety are keyin 
the design and operation of signalling systems (Thegg & Vlasenko 2009). 
From the risk management prospective, in the Swiss cheese model 
(Figure 1-2) signalling system represents a vital barrier between hazards and 
potential accidents. They protect against potential wrong human behaviours and 
contribute to enhance safety (Perrow 1984). As a result, passengers’ fatalities in 
railway operations are the lowest among all means of land transport and one of 
the lowest when compared with all the other kinds of transport (Beck, Dellinger 
& O’Neil 2007) (Ministero delle Infrastrutture e dei Trasporti 2009). 
 
Figure 1-2: The Swiss cheese model of accident causation. Source: 
http://www.fireengineering.com/content/dam/etc/medialib/fe/2010/march/5933.res/_jcr_conten
t/renditions/pennwell.web.600.530.gif [10 March 2015] 
1.3 Overview of Railway Operations, Headway and 
Capacity  
As mentioned in chapter 1.1.1, railway infrastructures are very 
‘inflexible’, meaning that overtaking and passing can normally only take place at 
==PAGE:31==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
RAILWAYS, SIGNALLING, AND LIGHT RAIL: GENERAL OVERVIEW 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         8 
                 
 
stations or loops. As a result, railway operations are constrained by a large 
number of restrictions and interdependencies.  
Because of that, timetables are crucial factors to guarantee optimal 
railway operations, as they are normally designed taking into both passenger 
wishes and operational needs. Consequently, due to the large amount of train 
operations that can be scheduled, the timetable should be strictly followed. If, for 
different reasons, deviations happen, delays will therefore often spread to other 
trains in the network. Therefore, it is considered a good practice to introduce 
buffers in the timetabling process. The aim is making possible for the railway 
system to regenerate in case of deviance from the timetabled operations (Landex 
2013).  
One of the main factors to be taken into account in timetabling is that on 
most railway lines, the signalling systems can still not determine the position 
and speed of the trains more precisely than the specific track block section. It 
means that traffic is only updated approximately once per minute. Therefore, the 
trains, in principle, occupy the individual blocks approximately 1 minute longer 
than strictly necessary. Moreover, the demand for sufficient braking distance and 
safety overlaps as well as robust and customer friendly timetable are other 
critical factors. In practical terms, this means that the minimum headway time (a 
measure of how closely two trains can run), corresponding to the maximum 
traffic density, on mono-directional line is circa 3 min/train.  Nevertheless, it is 
possible to obtain much smaller headway times, particularly on city lines 
(suburban trains or metros), where there is only one type of traffic and where 
the signalling systems are far more advanced (Landex 2013). The same applies 
for LR, where more rudimental signalling systems, if any, allow much shorted 
headway times, as further described in detail in chapter 2. 
A crucial factor in railway operations, that it is somewhat related with 
headway times, delays, and many more factors, is capacity. According to the 
international railway association (UIC), its definition is: “Capacity as such does 
not exist. Railway infrastructure capacity depends on the way it is utilized” (UIC 
2004). But many more definitions are found, one of them being: “The ability of 
the carrier to supply as required the necessary services within acceptable service 
levels and costs so as to meet the present and projected demand for such service” 
(Kahan 1979). 
Railway capacity is difficult to define because there are several 
measurable parameters that can affect it. Particularly, the numbers of trains, the 
traffic stability, and heterogeneity as well as average speed (see Figure 1-3) are 
dependent on each other and all affect capacity. In other words, railway capacity 
==PAGE:32==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
RAILWAYS, SIGNALLING, AND LIGHT RAIL: GENERAL OVERVIEW 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         9 
                 
 
is a balanced mix of various factors. LR has traffic characteristics similar to 
metros, that are indicated with the orange line in the following picture:  
 
 
Figure 1-3: Some of the main factors that contribute to capacity calculations and their 
interconnections. Source: UIC Code 406: Capacity. Available from: 
<http://banportalen.banverket.se/Banportalen/upload/1753/HandbokUIC406.pdf>. [10 March 
2015] 
It could be argued that the description of railway capacity presented by 
the UIC includes only the timetable and not the infrastructure, the rolling stock, 
the signalling system or the quality of service. However, it has to be noticed that 
those factors are implicitly included in the definition as they considered in the 
timetabling process. So, generally the inflexibility of the rail infrastructure is in 
itself an important capacity hindering factor (Landex 2013). 
1.4 Introduction to Light Rail 
The term Light Rail indicates the complex of civil infrastructures, rolling 
stock, and other related equipment designed and realized with the aim of 
optimally fulfilling urban mobility demand. Its capability of combing the 
technical advantages of railway transport (Chapter 1.1.1), with the ability to run 
in existing urban roads, represents LR’ unique feature (Grava 2003). Although 
several different definitions of LR exist, it can be described as: “Light rail transit 
is a metropolitan electric railway system characterized by its ability to operate 
single cars or short trains along exclusive rights-of-way at ground level, on aerial 
structures, in subways or, occasionally, in streets, and to board and discharge 
passengers at track or car-floor level” (TRB 1987). LR network indicates all the 
interconnected lines present in a given city and, usually, managed and operated 
by the same organization. LR vehicle (LRV) stands for the rolling stock employed 
==PAGE:33==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
RAILWAYS, SIGNALLING, AND LIGHT RAIL: GENERAL OVERVIEW 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         10 
                 
 
on LR networks. 
Even though modern LR shares some features and functionalities with the 
old concept of tram or streetcar, their difference came from a drastically re-
engineering process carried out at the end of last century. This was done with 
the aim of allowing high riding comfort and safety, enhancing running speed, 
reducing potential delays and improving operational reliability. The most 
significant different with the old streetcar and tramway concepts is that modern 
LR run, to a greater extend, in a segregated alignment (apart from intersections 
with roads). Besides, starting from the 1980’s, the construction of LR in many 
cities, especially the Mediterranean ones, represented a unique opportunity for 
re-designing urban spaces and enhance liveability of many suburban areas (UITP 
2001). 
 
Figure 1-4: Example of a modern LR in Nice, France, where the urban spaces have been enhanced 
thanks to the LR construction. Source: http://assets.inhabitat.com/wp-
content/blogs.dir/1/files/2009/01/europe-grass-LRV-railway-2-537x405.jpg [11 March 2015] 
From the transport stand point, modern LR has the peculiarities of having 
a medium-low distance between stops, a high amount of transportable 
passengers per time unit as well as a medium-high operational speed. Those 
features, combined with the fact that LRV are flexible vehicles that produce little 
noise and pollution, makes this urban transport means ideal for medium-big 
sized cities (UITP 2001). 
From the infrastructural standpoint, the main peculiarity of LR stands in 
extensive usage of grooved rail. This kind of rail have been developed to facilitate 
the installation of the rails embedded in road or, more generally, in ballast-less 
solutions. Grooved rail are characterized by the presence of lateral room for the 
wheel’s flange, known as flange way (TCRP 2012). 
 
==PAGE:34==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
RAILWAYS, SIGNALLING, AND LIGHT RAIL: GENERAL OVERVIEW 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         11 
                 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Another feature is the extensive use of with slab track, even if some lines 
are still realized with cheaper ballasted solutions. In order to enhance the 
implementation and the social acceptability of LR within urban areas, slab track 
are usually upholstered with grass or stones (TCRP 2012). 
 
Figure 1-6: Example with LR's slab track upholstered with grass and stones. Picture by Ranieri 
Brandani in Florence, LR line T1 [2 April 2015]. 
 
Moreover, newly build LRV and LR stations are usually realized in such a 
way that the height of the LRV’s floor and the platform coincide. This measure 
facilitates the entrance of people with reduced mobility, wheelchairs, and 
expedites boarding and alighting operations (TCRP 2012). 
Figure 1-5: In the left a comparison drawing between conventional Vingoles rail (blue) and grooved 
rail (green). Source: 
http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/TreinTramwielprofiel.svg/200px-
TreinTramwielprofiel.svg.png [11 March 2015] On the right, an example of a grooved rail embedded 
in grass in Florence. Picture by Ranieri Brandani [2 April 2015]. 
==PAGE:35==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
RAILWAYS, SIGNALLING, AND LIGHT RAIL: GENERAL OVERVIEW 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         12 
                 
 
 
Figure 1-7: Example of a LR having the station platform and the LRV floor at the same height. Picture 
by Ranieri Brandani in Florence, LR line T1 [2 April 2015]. 
From the energy standpoint, electrified LR (that are the absolute 
majority) has very high energy efficiency. In fact, modern electric engines can 
have efficiency up to 95%, compared with traditional diesel engines whose 
efficiency is around 25%. Moreover, considering all the inefficiencies of energy 
production and distribution and assuming that the energy employed for 
alimenting LRV comes from oil power plant; evidence show that starting from 
100 oil barrels, 22,8 are actually employed for the motion of a bus, whereas 47,8 
for moving an electrical LRV (Spinosa 2011). 
 
From the construction costs prospective, when compared with metro 
solutions LR enjoys construction costs 80% lower. In fact, the average 
construction cost for 1km of LR line is circa 40 Millions of Euros, while metros 
need about 210 Millions of Euros per kilometre of line. Performance wise, LR are 
able to reach circa the 80% of the operational speed and reliability of metro 
services (UITP 2011) (Gunduz, Ugur & Ozturk 2010). 
1.5 LR Brief Historical Evolution 
The history of LRVways begun on September 11th 1795 when in Crich 
(England) a LRV line was inaugurated. It was a 10 kilometres long line with 
horse-powered carriages running on fixed steel guide ways designed for 
passenger transport. The following year, another line of 12 kilometres was 
added, becoming the first LRV network in the World. This transport system was 
chosen after observing the rail system used in mines for freight transportation: a 
system where the strain for moving bulky items was minimized due to the low 
rolling friction between as well as being more comfortable and safe thanks to the 
tracks stability. In fact, the LRVway was an immediate success in terms of 
==PAGE:36==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
RAILWAYS, SIGNALLING, AND LIGHT RAIL: GENERAL OVERVIEW 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         13 
                 
 
passenger transported; hence this technology started to spread around the 
World (Brandani 2013).  
 
Figure 1-8: An old picture of a horse-powered LRV carriage in Trieste, Italy. Source: 
http://www.trasporti-fvg.it/images/LRVTS1.jpg [15 March 2015]. 
 
Some decades later, horse-powered LRVs started to be replaced by rolling 
stock powered by steam locomotives, the first example being Kassel (Germany) 
in 1877.  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Figure 1-9: Picture of a steam LRV locomotive in Milan. Source: 
http://www.repstatic.it/content/localirep/img/rep-milano/2015/03/13/180638654-38d443d3-
73c6-49b3-a724-2a5d77db2d53.jpg [15 March 2015]. 
Nevertheless, at that time the growth potential of LRVs for urban 
transport was recognized, but there were still inefficiencies in the traction 
mechanism. Hence, numerous researches were carried out, resulting in the 
creation of the first LRV with electric traction, open in Berlin in 1881. This new 
concept was a breakthrough, leading to a massive worldwide spread of this 
urban transport technology. This is proven by the fact that, at the beginning of 
==PAGE:37==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
RAILWAYS, SIGNALLING, AND LIGHT RAIL: GENERAL OVERVIEW 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         14 
                 
 
the 20th century, electrified LRVlines were built in more than 150 German cities. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
At that time, LRVs positively contributed in fuelling urban development: 
they enabled fast connections for urban masses between residential and working 
areas. The infrastructure was very basic; the rails were embedded in the roads 
that, at that time, were used to a larger extend by pedestrian and horse carriages 
only. The electric current, with low voltage, was provided through overhead 
wires. The use of signalling systems was extremely limited; LRVs were operated 
under a pure drive-on-sight regime and followed road’s rules and regulations.  
 
The maximum development of LRVs was achieved in the 1920’s, when the 
majority of European and North American medium and big sized cities had a LRV 
network. After this period, private cars started to spread and some LRV lines 
started to be closed down. In fact, at that time LRVs started to be considered 
hindering for cars, which were the modern, fast, and efficient mean of transport 
of the period. With exception of the communist block, the decline continued until 
the 60’s with LRVlines being continuously replaced with busses and metros.  
 
A drastic change in the public transport policies happened in the 1975 
with the first oil crisis, when the modern concept of LRV was engineered. It was 
at that time that the term Light Rail was invented, to indicate a revitalised LRV 
that can be an efficient solution for urban transport with enhanced features than 
its predecessors. So, LR started gathering popularity, especially in medium-big 
cities where, nowadays, light rails play a crucial role in urban mobility. The 
resurgence of LRVs and LR from the 1970’s after its almost universal demise in 
the first half of the 20th century, is one of the most remarkable turnarounds in 
transportation history (Kuby, Barranda & Upchurch 2004). 
Figure 1-10: An old electric tram in Milan. Source: 
http://storage.aicod.it/portale/teppr/crop/828/320/-61473-TRAM-15.jpg [15 March 2015]. 
==PAGE:38==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
RAILWAYS, SIGNALLING, AND LIGHT RAIL: GENERAL OVERVIEW 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         15 
                 
 
 
Figure 1-11: Example of a modern Light Rail. Picture by Ranieri Brandani in Florence, LR line T1 [2 
April 2015] 
In the USA, LR evolved from streetcar technology. Electric streetcars 
dominated urban transit in just about every significant American city up through 
World War II. But once the war was over, “old-fashioned” trolley lines were 
converted to bus operation, all in the name of the so-called modernization. By 
1965, only a handful of legacy streetcar systems still survived. 
The genesis of the terminology LR in the United States dates to the late 
1960s when planning efforts were underway at what was then called the Urban 
Mass Transit Administration (today’s Federal Transit Administration) to procure 
new vehicles for legacy trolley lines in Boston and San Francisco. The principals 
working on that program recognized that, because of the wholesale 
abandonment of streetcar lines in the previous two decades, the words 
“streetcar” and “trolley” had stigmas with likely negative political consequences 
for the program. Therefore, the term “light rail vehicle” was coined, borrowing 
from British vernacular (TCRP 2012). 
 
 
==PAGE:39==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: INTRODUCTION AND GENERAL FEATURES 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         16 
                 
 
2 LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: INTRODUCTION 
AND GENERAL FEATURES 
2.1 Main Purposes of LR Signalling 
LR signalling is a truly broad field, given its multiple purposes and the 
necessity to achieve diverse goals. In fact, the running safety, speed, line capacity 
and efficiency of LR are highly dependent by the signalling system. Broadly 
speaking, the objectives of LR signalling systems are to monitor and control the 
movement of the LRV for both safety and operational reasons. Where safety 
refers to the prevention of crashes and derailments on turnouts as well as rear-
ends collisions. While operational reasons refer to the optimization of LR 
operations and maintenance activities as well as to the assurance of reliable and 
high-quality services. 
LR Signalling System can be divided into two main areas (RARTJVa 2015): 
 
LR only signalling: this area is primarily involved in the prevention of 
LRV-LRV collisions, control and operation of turnouts as well as depots 
and maintenance centres; it is safety critical 
 
Intersection Priority Management: this area deals with LRV priority at LR-
road intersections; it is not necessarily safety critical. 
2.2 Drive-on-Sight and Conventional Signalling  
Even though the overall goals that need to be achieved by LR signalling 
systems – safety and operational efficiency - are the same as the ones pursued by 
conventional rail signalling, the functionalities of the two systems can be 
significantly different. In fact, the operating regimes of LR and conventional 
railway tend to be very different, requiring the implementation of peculiar 
system functionalities that meet specific operational requirement and support 
diverse goals. This leads to somewhat diverse signalling philosophies, working 
principles, equipment, and functionalities. Nevertheless, some LR and 
conventional rail signalling solutions share some basic technologies and working 
principles. 
One of the main purposes of conventional signalling is to supplement the 
driver’s sight with warnings indicating possible danger ahead. Thus, under 
conventional signalling regimes, a large part of the safety critical operational 
responsibilities are removed from the LR/train driver and placed on the 
signalling system (Thegg & Vlasenko 2009). Conversely, in the majority of LR 
==PAGE:40==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: INTRODUCTION AND GENERAL FEATURES 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         17 
                 
 
applications this is not the case. Here, the driver is responsible for the LRV 
running safety and the signalling equipment’s purpose is solely to provide 
additional help. This running regime is called drive-on-sight and it is feasible in 
LR contexts thanks to a number of peculiar features.  
 First, the main difference between the drive-on-sight and conventional 
rail operational regimes regards the maximum allowable vehicles’ speed. In fact, 
due to operational reasons and considering the average distance between stops, 
in the majority of LR application the maximum allowable speed is limited to 
70/80 km/h (depending by location, gradient, regulation, operator, and 
supplier). Whereas modern trains can reach speeds up to 360/400 km/h. This 
results in significantly different braking distances that, in the case of LR, are 
within few dozens of meters, while high-speed trains may require few kilometres 
before coming to a complete stop.  
Secondly, LR vehicles are lighter, more flexible and agiler than 
conventional rolling stock. Furthermore, they employ different braking systems 
technologies which further decrease braking distances. Those factors, together 
with the aforementioned speed restrictions, make LRV’s braking distances 
similar to the ones of comparable road vehicles, such as busses. 
As a result, the braking distance of a LRV travelling with speed up to 
70/80 km/h is expected to be shorter than the driver’s sight distance, with 
optimal sight conditions.  
 
 
 
 
 
 
This situation, in the majority of the cases, is opposite for conventional 
rail.  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
The drive-on-sight running mode is normally used by road vehicles and it 
is used in conventional rail only for degraded operation with very low speed. 
DRIVER SIGHT 
STOPPING DISTANCE 
Figure 2-2: Schematic representation of the typical situation where a running regime with 
conventional signalling is implemented 
DRIVER SIGHT 
STOPPING DISTANCE 
Figure 2-1: Schematic representation of the typical situation where a drive-on-sight running 
regime is implemented 
==PAGE:41==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: INTRODUCTION AND GENERAL FEATURES 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         18 
                 
 
Nevertheless, conventional signalling principles are, in some cases, used 
also in LR applications, where specific situations and running environments are 
found. Particularly, the most common locations where the safety-related risk of 
allowing a drive-on-sight regime may be either considered too high, or 
conventional signalling equipment could be needed to help the driver, are (: 
 
Turnouts and crossings 
 
Depots 
 
Stabling yards 
 
Bi-directional single track operations 
 
LR-road intersections 
 
Alignment allowing speed higher than 70/80 km/h 
 
LRV running on conventional railway (LRV-train) 
 
Low visibility areas due to tight curves, tunnel, bridges, etc. 
A more thorough description of those situations is given in chapter 4, 
apart from LRV-train that falls outside from the scope of the present thesis. 
From a risk management prospective, the risk involved in normal LR 
operations is considered to be not high. Accordingly, risk mitigation strategies 
through conventional signalling are needed only in limited circumstances. In 
other words, the risk related with the drive-on-sight regime is generally 
accepted in LR operations, in the majority of urban areas with double track LR 
operations. 
Finally, the railway capacity of LR lines is positively influenced by drive-
on-sight regime. In fact, consecutive LRVs are technically allowed to run closer to 
each other. Conversely, under conventional rail signalling regimes, trains run 
with higher headway distances due to the functionalities of block sections, which 
are not normally needed under the drive-on-sight regime. 
2.3 Light Rail Signalling Design in General 
The design of LR signalling systems is largely influenced by various 
operating environments. Since their differences’ range can be very large, the 
most appropriate level of signalling and automation varies according to LR 
operators’ requirements in terms of maximum speed and headways as well as 
the track configuration and alignment. These can vary significantly even along 
the same line. The optimum level of signalling functionality depends on such 
local circumstances, and it is generally determined by the LR operator, or its 
consultants. Engineering and designers are therefore requested to consider all 
the various signalling technology available to provide the desired safety and 
==PAGE:42==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: INTRODUCTION AND GENERAL FEATURES 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         19 
                 
 
system operating performance at the least total cost. In other words, LR 
signalling system design must consider not only what technology is available, but 
also the most economically and technically rational combination of equipment 
for a specific application. In fact, signalling systems are customized or specified 
by each LR operator to provide safe and efficient operations at an appropriate 
speed with the minimum cost.  
Due to the extensive use of drive-on-sight regime, dangerous situations 
have to be avoided as much as possible from the beginning of the LR planning 
and design stages, to keep both risk and signalling cost reasonably low. For that 
reason, it is suggested to follow “safety by design” principles in LR alignment and 
system project. This means that potentially dangerous situations have to be 
avoided starting from the design phase. Particularly, single track operations, low 
visibility intersections, tight curves have to be minimized as much as possible. As 
an example of safety by design, a layout track shaped as a roundabout can 
replace bidirectional single tracks operation in depots and yards.  
2.3.1 Light Rail Right-of-Way 
Other important factor to be considered in the design of the signalling 
system is the degree of separation between LR and road alignments. Broadly 
speaking, there are five types of LR right-of-way configurations, each of them 
having different signalling characteristics (TCRB 2012). 
2.3.1.1 In-street Mixed Traffic 
In this case the LR is operated with a drive-on-sight regime without any 
LR specific signalling system, other than traffic signals and possibly some basic 
intersection priority system. The allowable speed is set consistently with road 
traffic regulations. Consequently, LR drivers operate the vehicle as if they were 
operating a road vehicle on fixed guide way, obeying the applicable traffic 
regulations and, of course, being observant of the locations and movements of 
road vehicles and pedestrians. This solution is similar to the old concept of 
streetcar and it is not extensively used nowadays. 
==PAGE:43==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: INTRODUCTION AND GENERAL FEATURES 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         20 
                 
 
 
Figure 2-3: Example of a LRV running mixed with traffic. Source: 
http://www.railforthevalley.com/wp-content/uploads/2011/02/P8172895-street-running.jpg [20 
March 2015]. 
2.3.1.2 Semi-exclusive Right-of-way 
LR alignment is built within the street, but LRV runs in a semi-exclusive 
right-of-way physically separated from road lanes by curbing or fencing, except 
at intersections. In this case, conventional systems may be employed to enhance 
LR operations, including priority systems at intersections. However, LRV are 
usually operated with drive-on-sight principles, with LR signalling systems 
employed to protect switch operations at junctions and crossovers. It is the most 
common type of right-of-way used in modern LR. 
 
Figure 2-4: Aerial picture of a LR running on a semi-exclusive right-of-way in Madrid. Source: 
http://www.railforthevalley.com/wp-content/uploads/2010/01/1-madrid-light-rail.jpg [20 March 
2015]. 
==PAGE:44==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: INTRODUCTION AND GENERAL FEATURES 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         21 
                 
 
2.3.1.3 Exclusive Right-of-way 
In this case, LR operations are carried out in an exclusive right-of-way 
separated from potential intrusions. In some situation the maximum allowable 
speed is higher, therefore conventional signalling systems may be employed to 
avoid LRV-LRV collisions and with road vehicles crossing the tracks.  
 
Figure 2-5: Example of a LRV running with higher speed in an exclusive right-of-way in San Diego. 
Source: https://c1.staticflickr.com/9/8242/8474427431_c586de6e85_b.jpg [20 March 2015]. 
2.3.1.4 LRV-train  
In this case LR runs on conventional railroads. LRV have to be equipped 
with conventional signalling equipment and a drive-on-sight regime is generally 
unacceptable, hence the driver has to follow conventional railroad signalling 
rules. A detailed description of this configuration is not part of this thesis’s scope. 
 
Figure 2-6: An example of a LRV-train in Kassel. Source: 
http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/57/RBK_755_LRV-
train_approaching_Wolfhagen.JPG [20 March 2015]. 
2.3.1.5 Depots and Yards 
Operational rules and tight turnouts commonly restrict LR operations in 
==PAGE:45==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: INTRODUCTION AND GENERAL FEATURES 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         22 
                 
 
depots and yards to very low speeds. Generally speaking, busy turnouts are 
power operated and under the surveillance of the signalling system, while 
infrequently used ones are often hand thrown or with a spring. Signals are 
employed where required and its architecture can allow either vital or non-vital 
functions, and it may include track circuits.  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Figure 2-7: Example of a LR depot in Milan. Source: 
http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c6/2117_-_Milano_-

2.4 LR Signalling Regulatory Framework 
Worldwide, the regulatory situation in the field of LR signalling is diverse 
and it varies country by country, reflecting the different overall legislative 
frameworks present in the country. In some nations there are guidelines, in 
other normative or standards and in some others cogent laws. Even within the 
European Union, there are big national differences and somewhat different 
==PAGE:46==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: INTRODUCTION AND GENERAL FEATURES 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         23 
                 
 
philosophies in how the regulation are written and implemented. Nevertheless, 
there is an attempt from the EU to try to write common regulations with the 
upcoming Urban Rail Directive. Besides, the International Association of Public 
Transport issued some general designed guidelines.  
As a result, designer, engineers, and operators should familiarize 
themselves with the specific regulatory framework of the country where they are 
building or operating a LR line before starting any activity. In fact, different 
regulations may result in diverse design parameters, constraint, operating 
environment, and bureaucratic requirements to be fulfilled.  
For instance, in the UK there is no specific safety legislation for LR, but 
guidance for LRVway covering different areas is provided (ORR 2006). There, 
both infrastructure and rolling stock safety are assessed using the same criteria 
for heavy rail, but tailored for light weight, lower speed operation. 
Giving a detailed description of the various regulatory situations all 
around the World goes beyond the scope of the present thesis. However, it is 
worth to take a closer look at the German BOStrab, as progenitor of numerous 
regulations of other countries, especially north-European.  
2.4.1 The German BOStrab  
The term BOStrab comes from the German “Strassenbahn-Bau-und 
Betriebsordnung”, meaning “regulation on the construction and operation of rail 
transit systems”. In Germany, this regulation is an applicable law dealing with 
the design, construction, and operation of urban rail systems. It applies both to 
independent rail systems, like metros, and to street running systems, as old LRVs 
and modern light rails.  
BOStrab is a quite general regulation for the requirements it sets, leaving 
the designers, the manufacturers, and the operators some room to adapt the 
technical solutions to local circumstances. It is divided in eight parts (plus 
appendixes), which are: 
 
1st, General: Area of applications and general requirements 
 
2nd, Operating management: Main responsibilities and tasks of both the 
transport undertaker and the operations managers 
 
3rd, Operating staff: Requirements, training and conduct of the operating 
staff 
 
4th, Operational installations: General requirements of the permanent way 
as well as its related civil infrastructures and equipment 
 
5th, Vehicles: General requirements of the rolling stock and its main 
components  
==PAGE:47==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: INTRODUCTION AND GENERAL FEATURES 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         24 
                 
 
 
6th, Operations: It deals both with train operation and running safety as 
well as other operational issues, such as train staffing and maintenance. 
Signalling and priority management requirements are described in this 
chapter 
 
7th, 
Procedural 
formalities: 
Needed 
verification 
documents 
for 
construction or installation, supervision, and acceptance  
 
8th, Contraventions, Conclusions, Provisions, and Transitional Provisions: 
Rules regarding the entry in force of the regulations and the fines for the 
transgressors 
2.5 LR Signalling Historical Evolution 
Despite the fact that today’s LR signalling system cover both the area of 
intersection priority management and LR only signalling, their historical 
evolutions have been substantially different and their development separated. 
Only in recent years the two areas can be found within the same field of LR 
signalling and same overall functionalities as well as some equipment and 
systems are shared among the two areas.  
2.5.1 History of Intersection Priority Management Systems 
The first applications of Intersection Priority Management Systems 
(IPMS) date back to 1968, when an increasingly big number of European cities 
started implementing some sort of priority strategy at intersection, mainly for 
busses.  They were the response to growing traffic congestion and to the higher 
importance that were placed on policies supporting public transport. As a result, 
researches show that thanks to IPMS travel time was reduced for most of the 
routes by a minimum of 10% up to 50%. By the beginning of the 90s, IPMS were 
used in numerous European countries, such as Germany, Italy, United Kingdom, 
The Netherlands, France, and Switzerland (Hunter 2006).   
In America, the first signal priority experiment occurred in Los Angeles, 
USA, in 1970 and soon was followed by similar demonstrations. However, in the 
USA there were other problems associated with the system, regarding cross-
street queuing along the streets running the IPMS and their arteries. Accordingly, 
because of rapidly increasing vehicle demand queuing problems, there was a 
move toward elimination of IPMS. In fact, politicians drove a transportation 
policy that reverted to more efficient movement for all road vehicles versus a 
focus on transit vehicles only (Hunter 2006). 
IPMS started their big development in the early 1990s, primarily due to 
advancements in computer processing technologies and communication 
==PAGE:48==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: INTRODUCTION AND GENERAL FEATURES 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         25 
                 
 
capabilities were more efficient. This gave the traffic controller the ability to 
determine and implement adjustments to timing plans more efficiently.  
From a technological prospective, the first IPMS were created for busses 
and used inductive detector loops embedded in the road surface to detect transit 
vehicles. So, it is the infrastructure that communicates with the traffic signals 
which then actualize priority. The main problem was that the infrastructure was 
not able to effectively distinguish the vehicles to be prioritized. The next 
technological evolution employed vehicle-to-controller communications; fixed 
radio devices with antennas able to exchange information with in-vehicle balises 
were employed. Using this principle it was possible to give priority to the 
targeted vehicles only. The follow step, has been to use GPS receivers to locate 
targeted vehicles on the road network. The location information was then used 
to trigger the wireless transmission of a priority request from the vehicle to the 
nearby signal controller (Smith et al. 2005). 
The needs for LR priority come from similar reasons and pursue alike 
goals than the ones of busses priority. But the technological differences can be 
significant, mainly concerning the detector devices. Considering that LRVs run on 
reserved and fixed guide ways, it is easy to detect the targeted vehicles only. In 
the older systems, the communication between the various detector devices and 
other signalling equipment was wired. Those systems have been in used since 
many years and have proven to be efficient. But, as all the hardware equipment, 
they required special attentions and careful maintenance. Hence, in more recent 
years, there has been the need to develop innovative detector devices able to 
communicate wireless. Thus, it can be seen how LR and bus priority 
management technologies have, nowadays, come very close to each other 
(COWIO3 2015a). 
2.5.2 History of LR Only Signalling 
 
 At the beginning of the history of LRVs in the late 18th century, operations 
were carried out at very low speed with animal or steam locomotive. Under this 
circumstances, signalling as known today was not used. Hence, all the safety and 
operational related tasks were performed by human staff, turnout switching 
included.  
 
 In the second half of the 19th century, all basic principles of railway safety 
operation and signalling were developed, and a large majority of them are still in 
use today. At that time, basic form of mechanical interlocking, signals, and 
remote turnout control started to be implemented in railways. At the beginning 
of the following century, the first forms of electric detection with track circuit 
==PAGE:49==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: INTRODUCTION AND GENERAL FEATURES 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         26 
                 
 
combined with relay interlocking were invented and started to gain popularity in 
conventional rail applications. The most recent development in railway 
signalling has happened beginning in the 1980s, when electronic computer-
based interlocking systems started being widely used and having multiple 
positive implications in terms of safety, reliability, and line capacity. Besides, it 
has to be remembered that in conventional rail there has always been the need 
to achieve higher and higher operational speed and to transport larger amounts 
and weight of freight. Those two factors unavoidably lead to an increase of risk 
levels in rail operations, which had to be mitigated with advanced and up-to-date 
signalling solutions. (Thegg & Vlasenko 2009). 
 
 Conversely, in LR and LRVs operation there is not necessarily the urge of 
run with higher speed and to carry heavier loads. Therefore, risk levels have not 
been sensibly increasing overtime. Hence, there has not been the drive to find 
enhanced signalling solution to compensate higher risk levels in LR applications. 
On the other hand, it has been overtime proven that an adaptation of some 
conventional signalling solution for LR is crucial to achieve high operational 
performances as well as to enhance safety levels in those circumstances where 
operational risk is higher (Grava, 2003). 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:50==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: EQUIPMENT AND COMPONENETS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         27 
                 
 
3 LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: EQUIPMENT AND 
COMPONENETS  
3.1 Interlocking Systems 
This chapter deals with interlocking that, generally speaking, are 
arrangement of points, signals, and other safety critical devices interlinked in 
such a way that their status change, for instance a turnout movement of a signal 
aspect change, follows another in an adequate safe sequence (Theeg & Vlasenko 
2009). 
3.1.1 General Description 
Interlocking is the core system ensuring that LRV move in a safe manner, 
from the technical standpoint, where drive-on-sight is not allowed and trough 
turnouts. 
The interlocking performs its tasks by getting information about track 
occupancy, signal status, and the position of various track’s movable elements. 
After that, it evaluates the information and it issues movement authorities to the 
LRV via the signals as well as inputs to relevant trackside equipment, mainly 
signals and turnouts.  
Their designed start from to three basic principles. First, that a LR 
movement can be authorized only if all movable track elements are kept locked 
in the correct position, until the LRV clears them. Second, a LRV can enter a track 
section only if it is proven to be clear from other LRVs or obstructions. Third, 
interlocking functionalities and safety critical components have to be fail-safe. 
3.1.2 Technical Solutions  
Technically, there are three main interlocking technologies, which are 
described in the upcoming sections: mechanical, electric, electronic.  
3.1.2.1 Mechanical Interlocking 
Every safety critical component of the interlocking system has predefined 
physical characteristics that prevent errors, failures, and disturbances from 
occurring. Particularly, the main set features are the dimension and shape of 
components that preclude conflicting actions to be carried out in an unsafe 
manner. For instance, the movement of relevant commanding levers is 
mechanically locked, with interlocking devices such as tappets or drawbar, when 
a route is set trough a turnouts.  
==PAGE:51==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: EQUIPMENT AND COMPONENETS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         28 
                 
 
In detail, this interlocking is made up by mechanical levers and handles in 
the dispatcher control room, linked to trackside elements (turnouts, signals, etc.) 
with rods or wires. Therefore, the dispatcher needs enough physical strength to 
overcome friction and to move the turnout’s blades. Since the friction increases 
with the distance, mechanical interlocking is suitable to control limited areas, 
with the dispatcher control room nearby. Besides, due to the amount of movable 
parts, this interlocking needs high maintenance and the likelihood of failures is 
high. 
In LR applications, the use of this kind of interlocking is limited to old 
depots and yards. Nowadays, it is not used for newly built interlocking systems.  
 
Figure 3-1: Example of wires connected to a remotely controlled turnout (right, source: 
http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6e/Harris_Switch_Tower_interlocking.jpg) 
and handles of a mechanical interlocking (left, source: 
http://www.mrol.com.au/Gallery_Infrastructure/Signals/Western/Bewdley/IMG_498_Thumb.JPG). 
[3 April 2015]. 
3.1.2.2 Electric Interlocking 
Electric interlocking is also known as relay interlocking, since the main 
safety critical components are signalling relays (Figure 3-2). In those devices, 
electric current is used as binary code carrying information coming from the 
dispatcher, wayside elements, or detector devices.  
In the relay, the current attracts an armature that opens and closes one or 
more electrical contacts. The latter represent given positions or statuses of a 
defined field elements, which are operated and controlled remotely. 
==PAGE:52==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: EQUIPMENT AND COMPONENETS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         29 
                 
 
 
Figure 3-2: Drawing (source: http://www.electronics-tutorials.ws/io/io23.gif) and picture (source: 
https://developer.mbed.org/media/uploads/4180_1/300xNxrelay.jpg.pagespeed.ic.JikJkW9zXg.jpg
) of a common signalling relay [3 April 2015]. 
Operationally, the route can set locally or remotely both by using button 
and, in modern applications, a computer. The route setting operation energizes 
relevant relays, which accomplishes the command only if automatic trackside 
detector devices provide to the relays track clearance information and other 
safety critical information, such as the turnout’s position and the status of 
signals. Next, wayside elements are automatically set according to the set route 
and cleared once the LRV has passed.  
Its reliability is higher than mechanical interlocking, although more 
modern and complex applications can achieve even higher reliability levels. It is 
a relatively simple and cheap interlocking solution it is used in LR.  
3.1.2.3 Electronic Interlocking 
The most modern interlocking types are also known as computer 
interlocking, since the safety critical functions are carried out by programmed 
software running on specific hardware. Given the high complexity of hardware 
components and the unpredictability of software failure modes, risk mitigation 
measures have to be taken to achieve high safety levels. Particularly, almost 
every electronic interlocking uses the following measures: 
 
Hardware redundancy: the same functions are carried out and processed 
by multiple hardware and their result are compared  
 
Software diversity: Different software or operation systems are used in 
different hardware, which have to provide the same result. 
This interlocking technology is increasingly used, as hardware and 
software reliability has been increasing over the last years. It is a more expensive 
solution, which is preferred where higher LR operational reliability and safety is 
required.   
==PAGE:53==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: EQUIPMENT AND COMPONENETS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         30 
                 
 
 
Figure 3-3: A modern electronic interlocking. Source: 
http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/ce/Data_link_modules.jpg [3 April 2015]

3.2 Detection Devices 
Detection refers to the group of devices that get, transmit, and assess 
information about objects detected on railway lines. Their detailed description 
follows (Theeg & Vlasenko 2009).  
3.2.1 General Description 
Detection devices are employed to get information about the clearance 
status of the LR network. They are primarily designed to get the position of 
rolling stock along the line. It is of a particular importance to know where LRV 
are and whether a vehicle has reached a given point and has cleared it. They may 
also be employed to detect external objects of various natures, such as 
pedestrian, cars, or obstacles.  
 Detection can have safety related purposes or not. The first group refers 
to all those applications where a failure can cause accidents, whereas in the 
second one errors result in operational disturbances only. In LR applications, 
==PAGE:54==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: EQUIPMENT AND COMPONENETS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         31 
                 
 
non-safety related detection functions are more commonly used than 
conventional rail. In fact, in a drive-on-sight regime is the driver who performs 
the clearance detection of the track ahead. Where an interlocking is required, 
detection devices are necessarily connected to it. Vehicle detection is also needed 
in IPMS. 
There are three main groups in which detection devices are divided. First, 
they are divided among passive and active detectors; where in the first type 
individual LRV identification is not possible and in the second individual LRVs 
can be detected. The second category divides the parts of the rolling stock that 
are detected: 
 
Axle or wheels 
 
Rolling stock body 
 
LRV pantograph 
 
Active detection/communication devices mounted on the rolling stock 
The second group divides the part of the track that are subjected to 
detection: 
 
Point (or spot) detection 
 
Linear detection 
 
Area detection 
 
Spatial detection 
3.2.2 Detection Mechanisms 
The most common passive detection devices mechanism used in LR 
applications are described in the following sections. 
3.2.2.1 Spot Wheel Detection 
Spot wheel detection have been the first form of railway detection. 
Nowadays they are employed where the passage of a LRVs´ wheels have to be 
detected in a given point. There are various technologies, spanning from 
mechanical, to pneumatic, magnetic, and mass detectors. They are used in LR to 
get vehicle position information in key points, such as the beginning or finish of 
depots or terminals. They can also be used in temporary installations, thanks to 
their simplicity. They cannot be used where LR shares the alignment with road 
vehicles.  
==PAGE:55==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: EQUIPMENT AND COMPONENETS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         32 
                 
 
 
Figure 3-4: Example of a LR mass detection device in Dresden (source: http://www.hanning-
kahl.com/en/products-service/light-rail-technology/control-and-safety-technology/system-
components/vehicle-detection.html) [4 April 2015]. 
3.2.2.2 Track Loops 
Track loops are also known as track magnetic inductive loops and they 
are area detectors used to scan the vehicle body that passes over them. They are 
8-shaped resonant circuit situated in the middle of the track and their detection 
principle is based on the changing of inductivity due to the passage of LRV 
masses. They are largely used for intersection priority. They can also be used to 
detect road vehicles, if embedded in the asphalt.  
In the case that LRV carries a sender/transponder (such as VECOM) able 
to communicate with track loops, they can also have active detection properties. 
Particularly, when a LRV passes over them the LRV-based transponders 
communicate, usually via microwaves holding LRV ID information, with the loop, 
which then transmit the LR ID and positioning to other relevant systems.  
3.2.2.3 Track Circuit 
This linear technology for axle detection is the most used in LR 
applications where interlocking is needed as their working mechanism is 
relatively simple and reliable.  
Functionally, there is a low voltage electrical current feeder and a relay 
connected to an insulated portion of track, which creates an electrical circuit. 
When the track is clear, the circuit is entirely electrified and so is the relay. When 
a LRV passes, the steel wheels and axle shortcuts the circuit and the relay is no 
electrified, meaning that the relative track portion is occupied (see Figure 3-5). 
==PAGE:56==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: EQUIPMENT AND COMPONENETS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         33 
                 
 
 
Figure 3-5: Working principle of a track circuit (source: 
http://greatergreater.com/images/201003/012348.jpg) [4 April 2015] 
It is a very simple and low cost detecting solution that can also be used for 
broken rail detection. On the other hand, total insulation between neighbour 
tracks and with the surrounding is difficult to maintain. Besides, there are issues 
with returning current from the catenary and immunity against foreign currents, 
factors that may lead to a false clear status. For this reason, track circuit with 
audio frequencies can be nowadays employed to overcome those issues in LR 
applications. Nevertheless, the length of the track portion that can be detected 
with a single track circuit is limited to 1,5/2 km due to track electrical 
properties. However, in the majority of LR interlocking application this is not a 
big concern since drive-on-sight regime does not generally require long 
interlocked areas and LR lines are limited to few kilometres. In case of low-floor 
LRV without going-trough axles, the electrical connection between the wheels is 
done with a wire.  
As a peculiarity, in LR applications the following arrangement with 
shorted track circuit length can employed. There are two very short track circuit 
(whose length is less than a LRV) at every end of a potential bidirectional single 
track which are interlocked in such a way that the track is considered occupied 
when a LRV enters it, without the need to install a long track circuit all in the 
whole single track section, as can be seen in Figure 3-6.  
==PAGE:57==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: EQUIPMENT AND COMPONENETS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         34 
                 
 
 
Figure 3-6: Example of track occupancy with short track circuits 
The track is considered occupied (Figure 3-6, on the top) when the 
following steps happen: 
Step 
Entrance 1 
Entrance 2

After a LRV has entered the single track, it is considered unoccupied 
(Figure 3-6, on the bottom) when:

Table 3-3: Short track circuit occupancy steps, after a line block 
This solution is preferred in those circumstances where environmental 
conditions may represent a safety or an operational threat, as the risk of faulty 
shortcuts resulting in false occupancy increases. For instance, where the 
alignment is built into, or next to, roads subject to spread of salt in case of 
wintery conditions. Therefore, if short track circuit are employed the risk of 
shortcuts decreases. Moreover, the risk is further reduced if they are installed in 
specific locations far from possible pollution sources. 
3.2.2.4 Axle Counter 
It is an area mechanism that indirectly detects wheel passages. The 
system counts the LRV wheels that enters and exit a portion of track, which is 
considered to be free when the same number of wheels enter and exit it.  
==PAGE:58==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: EQUIPMENT AND COMPONENETS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         35 
                 
 
It is a system that is more reliable than track circuit but it is also more 
expensive; it does not have track block length constraint. Since track length is 
generally not a big issue for LR lines, its use in LR contexts is limited to train-LRV 
applications and where a more reliable interlocking solution is needed.  
 
Figure 3-7: Example of an axle counter: counting head (left) and digitaliser (right, the yellow box). 
Source: https://c2.staticflickr.com/4/3552/5714067212_e76b8a5fc7.jpg [4 April 2015]. 
3.2.2.5 Active Detection 
The other group of detection devices, that is increasingly gaining 
popularity in LR application, are active identification systems (Comune di 
Firenze 2014). In such systems, single LRV’s identity can be detected. Active 
detection is rarely used for safety critical applications. However, it is widely 
employed for optimizing operations, such as intersection priority management, 
dispatching, and network control. Besides, a link with the LRV on-board 
computer make possible to send more information along with the LR ID, such as 
the running status discrepancy according to the planned timetable. 
A big group of active detection devices are the continuous position 
measurement. The vehicle measures uninterruptedly its own position along the 
journey and it communicates it real time to the driver and, more importantly, to 
the CMC and to other relevant systems, such as ITCS and PSIS. Alternatively, the 
position is communicated only at pre-set location (e.g. stop line before 
intersection, station, etc.), called virtual loops, determined by the LRV on-board 
computer. This system is made possible thanks to recent development of data 
communication devices and their increased accuracy. Therefore, they are gaining 
popularity, especially considering that they do not require additional trackside 
signalling infrastructures. The two main LR continuous position measurement 
system are: 
 
Odometer or Doppler radar: the first system calculates the LR position by 
measuring wheels rotation, while the second employs microwaves 
reflection alteration (Doppler Effect). The distance is calculated relatively 
==PAGE:59==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: EQUIPMENT AND COMPONENETS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         36 
                 
 
to a fixed location and it has to be corrected at absolute fixed control 
points (such as balises, station, depot exit, etc.) to keep measurement 
errors within set boundaries and prevent their propagation. 
 
Satellite positioning: the LR-based receiver calculates its position with 
GNSS technologies with no need of fixed point of reference; hence this is 
an absolute system. The accuracy of such systems is generally low; 
therefore they are used only in control systems and, in some case, for 
sending intersection priority requests. They can be combined with 
odometer or radar systems in case of poor satellite coverage or total 
connection loss, e.g. in areas with plenty of tall buildings or within tunnels 
Another active detection system is the LR door opening detection devices. 
The system sends and input to the AVL when the LRV’s door are opened at the 
stops and terminals. The AVL knows the location of the expected LRV stops, 
meaning all the stations and the final terminals. Therefore, the LRV location is 
updated only when the LRV opens the door.   
It is a non-safety critical detection device that has the main advantage of 
being cheap. Its accuracy is related to the ability of the driver to stop the vehicle 
in the pre-set stopping position at every stop; overall a level of accuracy of circa 
1 meter can be expected. In LR contexts it is mainly used in the ITCS, PSIS, and 
for intersection priority management purposes.
