3.3 Turnouts 
Turnouts, described in this chapter, are track works engineered to split or 
merge a single track into two or more tracks or to provide connections between 
crossing tracks. Even though they are usually confused as synonymous of points, 
this is not strictly correct; in fact, the points are defined as all the movable parts 
composing a point. 
3.3.1 General Description 
Turnouts definitely represent an important, yet critical, part of the LR 
alignment. Their correct design, both from the infrastructure and the signalling 
standpoints, is crucial to achieve high LR performances. Figure 3-8 shows a 
schematic drawing of a possible turnout layout and indicates the name of its 
components. 
 
 
Figure 3-8: Schematic drawing of a turnout with indication of the components' name. Source:  Theeg 
& Vlasenko 2009. 
The transfer of rolling stock from one track to another is carried out by 
movable parts, called blades (2). The connection between the tracks is provided 
by the stock rails (3) and the connection rails, divided in straight (4) and 
α 
switching device, if any 
==PAGE:61==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: EQUIPMENT AND COMPONENETS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         38 
                 
 
diverging tracks (5). The guard rail (6) provides a safe guidance for the wheels 
flange while it runs in the discontinuities between the wing rails (7) and the frog 
core (8). But, as described in the previous chapter, a peculiarity of the LR is the 
shape of the rail profile. Thus, the guard rail function is provided by the outer 
part of the flange way of the grooved rail, which in some cases might be 
reinforced. All those elements can be seen in Figure 3-9 that shows an example 
of a LR turnout. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
The fouling point (9) represents the intersection of the rolling stock’s 
clearance profile of both tracks. In other words, it is the location that a LRV 
cannot pass without endangering movements on the other track. It is an 
important point for the design of the signalling system, as some safety critical 
distances are measured from this point. The fouling point can be either marked 
with a specific indicator or it can be virtual; the second option is the most 
common in LR applications.  
The angle α between the straight and the diverging track is called 
diversion angle and it is one of the most critical turnout design factors. It 
indicates the inclination of the diverging track referred to the straight track and 
it is a key factor in setting the turnout’s maximum allowable. In fact, the higher 
the angle is, the lower the turnout’s curve radius and, accordingly, the lower the 
allowable LRV speed result. Due to the typical application contexts of LR 
turnouts for LR can have diversion angles that are significantly higher than 
conventional railways. 
Figure 3-9: Example of a LR turnout with the reinforcement in correspondence of the frog location 
(highlighted in yellow). Source: http://allfortramtracks.com/wp-content/uploads/2013/10/Turnout-
w-curved-sec-in-branch-line.jpg. [10 April 2015]. 
==PAGE:62==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: EQUIPMENT AND COMPONENETS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         39 
                 
 
A LRV movement in the direction in which the tracks split is called a 
facing move, vice versa it is called trailing move. When a LRV is doing a trailing 
move from the wrong direction than the one in which the points are set, it will 
cause the blades to be moved by the wheels’ flange, causing the point to be 
trailed. Some types of switching devices are trailed in normal operations without 
any damage; others do not have this functionality.  
The element that in Figure 3-8 is called switching device (1) requires 
special attention and it is described in the following section. 
3.3.2 Switching Devices 
This is a core component of the whole turnout. In fact, turnouts need 
some sort of device to change the orientation of the blades and keep them in 
position. The switching devices can assume diverse forms and it can have 
different functionalities. Generally, it can be defined as a switching mechanism 
whose purpose is both to move and lock the blades according to the desired 
route requirements while safeguarding the rolling stock transit in the turnout 
area. There are three categories of such devices: 
 
Switch stands: They are manually operated devices with no direct 
connection to the LR signalling system. These are typically used only on 
seldom-used tracks that do not have any sort of signalling system or 
where there is no need to command the switch as the routes follow pre-
set patterns the majority of the times.  
 
Switch machines: They are switch-throwing devices that have a 
connection with the LR signalling system. In most cases, these are power-
operated devices although they usually incorporate a means of manual 
operation in the case of failures.  They may be controlled from a central 
control centre or by the vehicle operator. When time and remote control 
are important, automated switch machines are advantageous.  
 
Spring switches: Those switches’ blades are connected to a spring that 
moves the blades. They are typically used where the turnouts it is not 
necessarily connected to the interlocking or where there is not need for a 
switching machine due to the preferred LRV routes configuration. 
3.3.2.1 Manual Local 
The blades are moved locally and manually by the operator. It is a very 
simple and cheap solution but it requires a person’s physical presence and man 
power. Therefore, their usage in LR application is limited to rarely-used 
switches, for instance due to contingency of operations, depots, and shunting.  
==PAGE:63==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: EQUIPMENT AND COMPONENETS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         40 
                 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Figure 3-10: Left: An example of an old manually operated switch. Picture from Ranieri Brandani, 
Upper Peninsula, Michigan [12 October 2014]. Right: example of a modern LR manually operated 
switch in Dreseden http://www.hanning-kahl.com/en/products-service/light-rail-
technology/point-setting-systems/manual-point-machines.html [15 May 2015]. 
3.3.2.2 Electro-pneumatic 
The force required to move the blades is provided by compressed air. It is 
an automatic machine that can be remotely controlled and operated. The 
advantage is its small dimension, the disadvantage is the necessity of installing 
an air compressor which requires maintenance and tight air-humidity controls. 
The monitoring of the blade’s position is done electronically.  They are not 
widely used in modern LR applications. 
 
Figure 3-11: Picture of an old electro-pneumatic switching machine. Source: 
http://www.railwaymailservicelibrary.org/aftower/images/AFTOWR07.jpg [5 April 2015]. 
 
 
==PAGE:64==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: EQUIPMENT AND COMPONENETS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         41 
                 
 
3.3.2.3 Electro-magnetic 
Solenoid magnets are used to move the blades. It is a relatively compact 
and simple to maintain motor that can easily be implemented in LR applications. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
3.3.2.4 Electro-mechanical 
Electro motors generate the mechanical energy needed to move the 
blades. The force is transferred from the motor to the blades with a rack bar. The 
efficiency and reliability of those motors is high and they can also provide 
control functions. Hence, they are among the most widely used motors for point 
setting in LR contexts.   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Figure 3-13: Electro-mechanical switching machine (left, source: http://www.hanning-
kahl.de/fileadmin/_processed_/csm_HWE162_Lok_02_04ec6e301a.jpg [5 April 2015]) with a 
particular of the rack (right, source: 
http://www.westonlangford.com/media/photos/cache/111199_display.jpg [5 April 2015]) 
3.3.2.5 Electro-hydraulic 
As in the previous case, electro motors generate the mechanical energy 
needed to move the blades. But in this case the force is transferred from the 
motor to the blades with a piston filled with pneumatic oil. They are also widely 
spread in LR applications. 
Figure 3-12: Electro-magnetic switching machine: LR application (left, source: http://www.hanning-
kahl.com/fileadmin/_processed_/csm_Betrieshof-Gorbitz-Weichenantriebe-2_01_c0f755f0dd.jpg [5 
April 2015]) and particular of the machine (right, source: http://www.hanning-
kahl.com/fileadmin/_processed_/csm_point_machine_HW500_5_01_ab3ace2588.jpg [5 April 2015]). 
==PAGE:65==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: EQUIPMENT AND COMPONENETS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         42 
                 
 
 
Figure 3-14: Electro-hydraulic switching machine in a LR application. Source: http://www.hanning-
kahl.de/fileadmin/_processed_/csm_61.1_Dresden_7eae469b8d.jpg [5 April 2015]. 
3.3.2.6 Sprung Switch 
Those turnouts do not have any motor that moves the blades.  The blades 
are set in one direction, but the spring permit a trailing movement and 
automatically reverts them to the original direction after the wheels have passed. 
The standard blade position is set and cannot be remotely changed, apart from 
some cases that can be locally changed. They are widely used in LR applications 
where turnouts do not have the necessity of being remotely controlled and 
commanded. Hence, they are installed where the large majority of the routes 
traversing the turnout follow the same pattern.  
Usually, there is an additional hydraulic dumper that prevents the impact 
of the blades against the rail in the returning movement. This is useful in urban 
environments where noise and vibrations prevention is key as well as it eases 
maintenance. This mechanism is easy to maintain and it enjoys low equipment 
and installation costs.  
 
Figure 3-15: Example of a embedded LR track with a spring point. Source: 
http://allforLRVtracks.com/wp-content/uploads/2013/10/Turnout-w-curved-sec-in-branch-
line.jpg [4 April 2015]. 
==PAGE:66==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: EQUIPMENT AND COMPONENETS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         43

3.3.3 Turnout Layouts 
According to the track geometry, turnouts assume various layouts; the 
most common are presented in the following sections (Thegg & Vlasenko 2009).  
3.3.3.1 SimpleTurnouts 
They split a single track into two (single turnout) or three tracks (double 
turnout) or join diverse tracks into a single one. They are commonly used in 
single track lines and in stations areas. 
 
 
Figure 3-16: Layout of single lateral turnout (top left), single equilateral turnouts (top 
right) and double turnout (bottom). Source: http://www.tsb.gc.ca/eng/rapports-
reports/rail/2007/r07q0001/images/r07q0001_appendix_a.gif [6 April 2015].

3.3.3.2 Diamond Crossings 
It is used to traverse two tracks without the possibility of shifting from 
one track to the other one. It is found where two LR alignment cross each other.  
 
 
Figure 3-17: Layout of a diamond crossing. Source: 
http://www.dccwiki.com/images/b/b6/Crossing.png [6 April 2015]. 
3.3.3.3 Slip Crossing 
It is a diamond crossing that allows one (single slip) or two (double slip) 
changing of tracks. It is found where two alignments cross each other and the 
possibility of track changing it has to be allowed. They are difficult to build and 
maintain, but they are a very compact switching solution. Therefore, their ideal 
application context is in terminals and in urban LR intersections.  
 
Figure 3-18: Layout of a double (left) and single (right) slip crossing. Source: 
http://www.dccwiki.com/images/a/aa/CrossingSingleSlip.gif [6 April 2015]. 
3.3.3.4 Crossovers 
They are used to connect two parallel tracks. They can provide 
connection in one direction (single crossover) or in two directions (double 
crossover). The blade’s positions can be interlocked in order to avoid conflicting 
routes in parallel tracks. They are largely used in LR applications. 
 
==PAGE:68==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: EQUIPMENT AND COMPONENETS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         45 
                 
 
 
Figure 3-19: Layout of a single (top) and double (bottom) crossover. Source: Theeg & Vlasenko 2009. 
3.3.3.5 Ladder 
It is a layout that consists of a combination of crossovers and turnouts 
that are set in such a way that a single track is split into three or more parallel 
track. It is particularly used in depot areas. 
 
Figure 3-20: Layout of a ladder. Source: Theeg & Vlasenko 2009. 
3.3.4 The Issue of Frog Core’s Gap 
A big issue with turnouts, that has consequences on the design of the 
turnout and the signalling system, is represented by the rail gap that there 
naturally is in the frog area, as shown in Figure 3-21 (Kerr 2003). 
 
Figure 3-21: Example of a simple turnout's frog, where the gap between the rails is evident. Source: 
http://www.rrpicturearchives.net/pictures%5C52713%5C100428002.jpg [7 April 2015]. 
==PAGE:69==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: EQUIPMENT AND COMPONENETS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         46 
                 
 
 This gap can cause problems in the infrastructure, in the vehicle, and in 
the surroundings of the turnout area. In fact, when the wheels pass the turnout, 
they impact on the frog or on the rails due to the gap. As a result, due to repeated 
impacts, both the wheels and the tracks may be damaged. Besides, the area 
surrounding the turnout is affected by the repeated vibrations and noises 
coming from the numerous wheels’ impacts. This latest question is specifically 
important for urban LRs, where noise disturbs people and vibrations cause 
problems to buildings.  
There are various technical solutions to overcome this issue, which 
requires different signalling equipment. They are analysed in the following 
sections. 
3.3.4.1 Movable Frog or Swingnose Crossing 
This solution eliminates the physical gap between the rail and the frog. 
There is a motor that moves the frog point according to the blade position, 
resulting from the set LRV route. Therefore, a form of interlocking needs to be 
installed (Ping & Fuguang 1999).   
Movable frogs are commonly used in metro and heavy rail lines to reduce 
vibration in the civil infrastructures, but they can be found in LR too. They are 
employed where the usage of a route direction significantly prevails over the 
other one and where the angle of diversion of the diverging track is low. It is a 
very effective solution, but the motor needs to produce a lot of force to move the 
point and it requires additional maintenance. It cannot be used in solving the gap 
in diamond crossings. 
 
Figure 3-22: Turnout with a movable frog. Source: 
http://www.voestalpine.com/bwg/static/sites/c015/images/products/ehfb.jpg_613543918.jpg. [7 
April 2015] 
3.3.4.2 Running on Flange 
In this solution the physical gap is not eliminated but the wheels’ impacts 
with the frog and the rail are prevented. This is achieved by letting the wheel 
==PAGE:70==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: EQUIPMENT AND COMPONENETS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         47 
                 
 
running on flanges to overpass the gap. Small ramps are realized in the 
flangeway in order to lift the wheels when they approach the gap and lower 
them after the gap. So, the impacts with the rail infrastructure are elimiated. 
This solution is easy to implement, especially in case of grooved rail, and it 
does not require additional maintenance, since there are not machines needed to 
move the frog point. Besides, it does not require any additional signalling 
equipment and interlocking. 
It is the most commonly used solution in LR applications and it can also be 
used in case of diamond crossings. Running on flange is not allowed where the 
allowable running speed is higher than 70/80 km/h, as the risk of derailment 
due to possible flange flying after the frog’s core increases (Sun, Cole & 
McClanachan 2010).  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Figure 3-23: Example of a LR turnout where the wheel runs on flanges. In the right side 
there is a particular of the wear on the flangeway due to wheels running on flange. Picture 
by Ranieri Brandani in Florence, LR line T1 [2 April 2015] 
3.3.5 Gauntlet Tracks 
Turnouts are also a very critical part of the LR alignment in terms of 
installation and maintenance. In fact, there are various movable parts, 
sophisticated electromechanical equipment, and track pieces that require 
particular attention. With the aim of reducing turnout’s maintenance, some 
special track arrangements are set, and have an impact on the signalling system 
(Hay 1982).  
The most relevant example in LR applications is gauntlet tracks. Thanks 
to the peculiarity of LR that uses grooved rails, it is easy, in some cases, to realize 
==PAGE:71==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: EQUIPMENT AND COMPONENETS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         48 
                 
 
such a configuration (Figure 3-24). That is, tracks are laid parallel and 
overlapped in such a way that only one couple of rails are used at a time. This 
eliminates movable blades and point machines, moreover two tracks share some 
infrastructure parts (substructure, sleepers, overhead catenary, etc.); factors that 
ease maintenance and make this a cheaper solution. It is used in particular 
where there are lateral turnouts and the LR alignment’s geometrical 
configuration is complicated (e.g, where there is a bottleneck due to buildings). 
From the signalling and traffic prospective it still is a single track 
operation, but turnouts do no need to be installed to overcame complicated 
layouts. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
3.4 Signals and Semaphores 
Signal and semaphore are the most elementary, in rail terms, form of 
communication between the signalling system and, primarily, the LRV driver. 
3.4.1 General Description 
The main purposes of LR signal and semaphores are to convey 
information and instructions to people involved in or affected by LR operations. 
The first category of people mainly refers to LRV drivers and LR staff in general, 
while the second mostly refers to road users, pedestrians, bikers, and passengers 
that come close to the LR alignment or vehicles (Theeg & Vlasenko 2009). 
Overall, the signals have the following features: 
Figure 3-24: Example of two alignments realized with gauntlet tracks in Amsterdam. Source 1: 
https://austinrailnow.files.wordpress.com/2013/10/arn5_ams-lrt-rte10-interlaced-trk-temp-bridge-
hoge-sluis-20120303.jpg [15 April 2015]. Source 2: 
https://austinrailnow.files.wordpress.com/2013/10/arn4_ams-lrt-leidsestraat-interlaced-trk-peds-
20070519_revo-arka-giri-soekatno.jpg [15 April 2015]. 
==PAGE:72==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: EQUIPMENT AND COMPONENETS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         49 
                 
 
 
They and their indications have to be easily and quickly recognizable  
 
They have to convey explicit messages 
 
Their conveyed information should always be shown in the same way in 
different circumstances along the same LR network 
 
Their aspects have to be easy to memorize 
 
The signal information has to be given neither too early, to avoid the risk 
of forgetfulness, nor to late, to avoid signal overriding 
 
They have to be designed and realized fail-safe, meaning that a failure 
should always lead to shown a safe aspect 
 
They have to be economically efficient, reliable, easy to install, maintain, 
and repair. 
The most common signal aspects that need to be conveyed to the LR 
drivers are: 
 
Stop signals: It is not allowed to proceed any further 
 
Proceed: It is allowed to proceed  
 
Speed restriction: temporary or permanent speed restrictions have to be 
obeyed by the LR driver 
 
Route indication: information about the route that is set trough a turnout 
Signals can be classified according to various criterions, the most common 
classifications are done according to the: 
 Human senses involved: Information can be conveyed by using optical 
and/or acoustic signals 
 
Location of signals: They can be fixed along the LR alignment (trackside 
signals), on the exterior of the LRV (vehicle signals), or directly in the 
diver cab (cab signals) 
 
Signal technology: Signal can be conveyed from a person (hand signals), 
from different position of objects (mechanical signals), by different light 
arrangements and colours (light signals), or by a display directly to the 
driver (cab signalling) 
 
Positive or negative signals: Signals are positive when the information is 
given by the presence of the signalling device, whereas in negative signals 
the information is given by the absence of the indication 
 
Ability to switch status: Some signals always have the same status (fixed 
signal) whereas others can switch between different aspects (switchable 
signals) 
 
Signal aspect formation: Switchable signals can be formed by different 
==PAGE:73==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: EQUIPMENT AND COMPONENETS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         50 
                 
 
colours (colour light signals), by different arrangements of light of the 
same colour (position light signals), or a combination of the two (colour 
position signals) 
3.4.2 LR Signals  
LR signals can be used to indicate the LRV driver whether the it has the 
authority to undertake a given movement. There are mainly two different 
contexts where signals are used. The first one in case of LR-road intersections. 
The other is used for LR only indications in presence of, for instance, turnouts, 
single track portions, depots, and similar circumstances – where some sort of 
interlocking is generally needed. 
Nowadays, the most common signal types employed in normal LR 
operations are positive light signals, with a combination of optical and acoustic 
signals. There usually is a mix of switchable colour and position signals as well as 
fixed signals placed trackside, the vehicle and, in limited cases, in the diver’s cab. 
For LR-road intersections, even though it is technically feasible that LRV 
use the same traffic lights as road, there are various reasons why LR has its own 
signals. The first is due to the theory of the amber time.  This theory states that if 
a traffic signal changes to amber as a vehicle approaches, the driver has the 
choice between continuing or of stopping before reaching the stop line. But, 
considering that road vehicles and LRVs have different braking decelerations; 
they would require different amber time to stop safely within the stop line 
(Gazis, Herman & Maradudin 1960). The other reason is that LR intersection 
priority does require that traffic signals are targeted to different vehicle 
categories (Miur 1989). 
In both cases it is essential to avoid situations where it is unclear to which 
category the signals are addressed at. In most countries this is achieved by using 
white rather than coloured signals, with bars rather than coloured circles. But 
this varies country by country. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Figure 3-25: Two examples of LR traffic lights: British (right, source: 
http://www.drivingtesttips.biz/images/tram-light-signals.jpg [16 April 2015]) and Italian (left, 
source: http://www.scae.net/public/catalogo/futura%20tram.jpg [16 April 2015])

The other group of signals is used in LR only areas. Those signals require 
communicating to the LRV driver specific LR operational and safety information.

3.4.3  LR Signalling-Related On-Board Equipment 
Generally, the signalling related equipment that are commonly found a on 
modern LRV are (VDV 2014): 
 
Elaboration unit (also known as IBIS): it is an on-board computer that 
stores multiple operational info and can calculate various crucial 
information, such as LRV position, running status, and many more. It is an 
interface between the driver cab equipment, the LRV diagnosis system, 
various radio and communication devices, detection devices, and the 
transponder/sender. 
 
Driver displays: In every cab there are a monitors and a keyboard allowing 
the driver to interact with the IBIS when required, for instance in setting 
the route and timetables.  
 
Figure 3-27: LR signals indicating switches position. Source: 
http://www.bathtram.org/tfb/Signals.gif [15 April 2015]

==PAGE:76==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: EQUIPMENT AND COMPONENETS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         53 
                 
 
 
Figure 3-29: Example of a LRV driver cab with several displays. Source: 
http://www.worldcharts.co.uk/images/metro/flexity02.jpg. [16 April 2015]. 
 
Active LRV communication box: underneath every LRV cab there is a box 
whose task is to actively communicate data between the LRV and the 
active trackside equipment, and vice versa. They have an interface with 
the IBIS  and with the on-board radio transponder. It is commonly known 
as VECOM. It communicates route information active devices in the 
infrastructure. The route information can be automatically set by the IBIS 
at the beginning of the journey, or the driver can command the position of 
the upcoming turnout via a joystick in the cab.  
 
Sand and magnetic brake: In order for the LRV to completely stop before 
the stop line, especially when friction is particularly low (wet rails, leaves, 
ice, etc.), a special device release sand in front of the first LRV wheels. By 
doing so, the coefficient of friction is locally increased and the vehicle can 
stop more efficiently, minimising the risk of overrunning the stop line.. 
Moreover, magnetic brakes can be used both for stabling and for 
emergency stop.

4 LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: MAIN 
APPLICATION CONTEXTS 
4.1 Turnouts 
As pointed out in chapter 3.3, turnouts are key elements to be taken into 
account in the design and implementation of LR signalling systems. The purpose 
of this chapter is to provide a deeper analysis of some typical application 
contexts for turnouts: terminals and track crossings.  
Generally speaking, turnouts are needed to allow LRV change of track and 
possibly direction before and/or after terminals and stops and in other strategic 
locations along the LR alignment. Even in case of lower speed, when multiple 
routes are potentially conflicting trough a turnouts, some interlocking 
functionalities are required. Mechanized switching machine in interlocked area, 
are generally commanded by the driver trough a joystick placed in the driving 
cab, or by pre-set route information stored in the IBIS or in the ITCS.  
As acquisition and maintenance costs for turnouts and the related 
signalling are relatively high, the aim is to minimize their installation to where it 
makes operational sense to have signalized turnouts (TCRP 2012). 
4.1.1 Turnouts at Terminals 
Terminals are the final station of a given LR line, where the vehicles 
generally need to reverse the running direction, besides allowing passengers 
boarding and alighting. LR terminals features three main configurations: with a 
roundabout, with a double crossover or with a single one.  
4.1.1.1 With Roundabout 
At the end of the line there is a track loop that allows LRV to change 
direction without requiring the driver to switch cab. This solution does not 
require any turnout and, potentially, LRVs can be equipped with one driving cab 
only. On the other hand, it does require large spaces to be realized, which do not 
make this the ideal solution for densely built urban spaces. 
Passengers usually alighting on one platform and board on the other. 
Waiting and spare vehicles are kept on the loop, or in a specific stabling track 
connected to it. 
==PAGE:79==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: MAIN APPLICATION CONTEXTS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         56 
                 
 
 
Figure 4-1: Air picture of a terminal with roundabout. Source: 
http://www.trz.cz/images/LRVvaj_konecna.jpg [19 April 2015] 
4.1.1.2 With Double Crossover 
In this case, there is a double crossover before the terminal. LRV can use 
either of the platforms, according to their clearance status. The LRV has to use 
the crossover either before the arrival at the terminal or after the departure from 
it. Both the boarding and alighting of passengers happen at the same platform, as 
well as the dwelling time. This solution is more compact than the other one, but 
it requires a double cross-over with at least one switching machine. In case of 
trailing movements, there can be a spring that allows such movements without 
the necessity for the switching machine to move the blades, saving maintenance. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Figure 4-2: Sketch of a terminal solution with double crossover. The two possible directions are 
highlighted in green and blue. The type of preferred switching mechanism, in case of right hand 
driving, is also reported in the figure. 
 
TERMINAL

Spring

Spring 
==PAGE:80==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: MAIN APPLICATION CONTEXTS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         57 
                 
 
 
Figure 4-3: Picture of a terminal solution with a double crossover. Picture by Ranieri Brandani in 
Florence, LR line T1 [2 April 2015]. 
4.1.1.3 With Single Crossover 
The second solution is made up by a single crossover after the terminal’s 
platform. The LRV always arrives at the same platform, it alights the passengers 
and it precedes a bit further the terminal and it trails the single crossover. After 
that, the driver switches cab and the LRV proceeds to the other platform via the 
crossover. It next can board passengers and proceed in the other direction. This 
solution does not require any switch machine, as a trailable spring point could be 
enough.   
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Figure 4-4: Sketch of a terminal solution with a single crossover. The possible direction is 
highlighted in green.

4.1.2 Crossovers 
Potentially, LR stops in the middle of the line do not necessary need any 
turnouts. They may be installed in two cases: when stops are also terminals for 
limited LRV runs or to allow routing flexibility in case of contingency of 
operations.  
In both cases, the LRV can reverse direction before or after the turnouts, 
as shown in the following pictures. In order to keep signalling cost low, the 
design tends to minimize the use of point machines while using as much trailable 
spring points or manual switching devices as possible.

LR line T1 [2 April 2015]. 
In the case of contingency of operations, the crossovers have to be placed 
in strategic location. Their positioning is carefully decided after modelling, 
studying, and optimizing recovery strategies due to various operational 
disturbances or disruptions.  
4.1.3 Crossings 
Where two or more track cross each other, both in case of diamond and 
slip crossings, conventional signalling solutions may be required to guarantee 
flank protection if the sight is limited and/or the speed is high. Detection devices 
before and after the crossings get the clearance status of the crossing, with the 
interlocking systems responsible for assessing the clearance information and 
setting proper signals. Conventional signalling may be omitted where there is 
enough visibility and the LRV speed is appropriately low, making possible for the 
driver to safely stop before occupying the crossing. This is usually not the case in 
urban areas. 
==PAGE:83==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: MAIN APPLICATION CONTEXTS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         60 
                 
 
 
Figure 4-7: Example of a LR diamond crossing. Picture by Ranieri Brandani in Florence, LR line T1 [2 
April 2015]. 
4.2 Depots and Maintenance Centres 
Every LR network has to be linked to one or more depots and 
maintenance centre (CMC). Those are crucial facilities to achieve high LR 
operational efficiency, as the place where fleet maintenance plans are carried 
out, where vehicles are safely kept when out of service, and where they are 
prepared for daily operations. It is a very busy part of the LR network which has 
to be well planned, designed, built, and maintained. Accordingly, considerations 
about routes within the CMC, its signalling systems, its storage capacity, and the 
maintenance duties to be carried out are fundamental issues (VDV 2014).  
The most common routes that are followed within the CMC, that are of 
course related with the signalling systems, can be grouped in the following 
(RARTJV 2015a): 
 Entry → washing or maintenance → stabling 
 Entry → stabling  
 Stabling → washing or maintenance → stabling 
 Stabling → exit 
 Stabling → washing or maintenance → exit 
 Entry → washing or maintenance → exit 
Taking into account the aforementioned routes, the size of the LRV 
storage areas, the dimensions of the maintenance facilities and, more 
==PAGE:84==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: MAIN APPLICATION CONTEXTS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         61 
                 
 
importantly, the track geometrical layout within the depot have crucial 
consequences on the design of the CMC signalling system. Safety by design, as 
explained in chapter 2.3, is largely used in CMC areas to keep signalling 
equipment to a minimum level. If those principles are not followed, the overall 
functionalities and architecture of LR depot signalling can quickly become very 
similar to conventional signalling, adding system complexity and cost, as the 
number of turnouts is high and so are conflicting routes.  
4.2.1 Depot Design 
A modern LR CMC should be operated with minimum personnel, 
signalling equipment at low cost and be as mechanized as possible. However, an 
optimal level of LRV detection and signalling systems ensure efficient and safe 
operations on the depot.  
Although local considerations have to be taken into account, the following 
overall CMC design principles can be followed:  
 
The tracks and the turnouts should be planned and arranged in such a 
way that the most frequently used routes are operated directly, without 
shouting, and, possibly, by limiting the number of turnout switching 
machine. This is an advantage since it both reduces the amount of 
turnouts needed and the number of times they are used.   
 
Replace as much as possible bidirectional single tracks with circular track 
loops around the CMC. The aim is to create a sequential clockwise (or 
counter-clockwise) arrangement of facilities and stabling areas so that 
every LRV enters the depot, performs its washing and maintenance tasks, 
go to the stabling area and exiting the depot without changing direction, 
as shown in Figure 4-8. This solution creates a seamless vehicle flows’ 
within the CMC area and, as a result, it eases shouting and drastically 
reduces the time LRV spend manoeuvring, which increases operational 
efficiency. And, more importantly, single track bidirectional operations 
are not carried out during normal operations. This generally means that a 
drive-on-sight running regime can be established in large part of the CMC 
area. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:85==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: MAIN APPLICATION CONTEXTS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         62 
                 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Figure 4-8: Example of a LR Depot and Maintenance centre with a clockwise sequence of 
facilities. Source: Google Maps [20 April 2015]. 
 
Use as much spring points as possible and set the most frequently used 
routes accordingly. In the case that is unavoidable that a turnout has to 
change position frequently, remotely controlled electro-mechanical 
switching mechanisms have to be preferred.  
Accordingly, the entrance of the LR CMC can be organized as in Figure 4-9. 
So, the entrance and the exit favours a counter-clockwise LRV circulation in the 
CMC. Besides, the exit’s turnouts EX1 and EX2 can be spring, whereas the 
entrance ones are, very likely, remotely controlled and under the surveillance of 
the signalling system. 
==PAGE:86==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: MAIN APPLICATION CONTEXTS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         63 
                 
 
 
Figure 4-9: Drawing of a possible entrance of a LR CMC 
4.2.2 Detection, Command, and Depot Management System 
LRV route setting and the command of some turnouts within the CMC 
needs to be by the dispatcher, who can get the status of the entire CMC facility on 
graphs showing vehicle detected by the signalling system. The detection system 
is able to recognize single vehicle identification numbers. The final signal 
processing takes place in the signalling system and finally in the Depot 
Management System (VDV 2014). 
A DMS is a non-safety critical system that helps the dispatcher and the 
operators by automatically performing the following tasks, with the aim of 
increasing operational efficiency: 
 
Planning, monitoring and control of LRV arrival and departure within the 
CMC area 
 
Monitoring and co-ordination of maintenance and washings tasks  
 
Monitoring and visualisation of vehicle movements and stabling in the 
depot 
 
Controlling, elaborating, and supplying of various relevant vehicles data 
to the ITCS and AVL system. 
A stylised representation of the depot including the most important 
buildings, tracks and vehicles is used for display of vehicles und vehicle 
locations, as shown in Figure 4-10 (Wandslebe et al. 2006). 
==PAGE:87==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: MAIN APPLICATION CONTEXTS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         64 
                 
 
 
Figure 4-10: Visualisation of vehicle locations and vehicle status, part of a DMS monitor. Source: 
http://www.hanning-
kahl.com/fileadmin/_processed_/csm_VABdepot_Disposition_Vorderseite_Bild05_01_e77647056a.jp 
[20 April 2015]. 
4.3 Line Blocks 
Line blocks are limited part of tracks that are under the surveillance of 
the signalling system. This system’s task is to guarantee safety against following 
and opposing LRV movements on open lines. Every block can be occupied at 
most by one LRV, its braking distance, and safety margins. Detection devices are 
employed to check the clearance status of the tracks within the line block, 
interlocking system asses those information, and equipment such as signals, ATS, 
and turnouts react accordingly.  
Generally, in LR line blocks are employed where a drive-on-sight regime 
cannot be established with appropriate safety levels, and conventional signalling 
is needed. Even though some specific local circumstances may require line 
blocks, there are some general cases where they are commonly used in LR lines, 
which are described below. 
4.3.1 Higher Operational Speed 
In all those lines, or their parts, where the operational speed is higher 
than 70/80 km/h (depending according to the country and the operational 
settings), line blocks are needed. This is due to the fact that the driver sight 
distance is expected to exceed the LRV’s stopping distance; therefore signals are 
needed to communicate in advance the clearance status of the part of track 
ahead. They are generally found in extra-urban alignment with few stops.  
==PAGE:88==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: MAIN APPLICATION CONTEXTS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         65 
                 
 
 
Figure 4-11: Example from San Diego (USA) blueline, where LRV run at 90 km/h conventional level 
crossings and signalling are needed. Source: 
ttp://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Blue_Line_(San_Diego_Trolley)#/media/File:Sandiegotrolley.jpg [21 
April 2015]. 
4.3.2 Low Visibility Areas 
Tight curves with lateral sight obstacles, alignment within tunnels, and 
generally all those specific locations where the driver sight is limited, line blocks 
need to be installed. In fact, the driver needs to accurately know that the hidden 
track beyond the visual obstacle is free. If this information is not available to the 
driver, the speed should be reduced. Such circumstance might negatively affect 
the system’s operational performance.  
 
Figure 4-12: LR alignment with a tight curve in a underpass: in such a location line block may be 
required. Source: Google Maps [21 April 2015]. 
==PAGE:89==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: MAIN APPLICATION CONTEXTS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         66 
                 
 
4.3.3 Single or Gauntlet Track Operations 
Even though single track bidirectional operations or gauntlet tracks have 
to be avoided as much as possible, in some cases they are necessary. This may be 
due to geographical constraints (few space on streets due to building), entrance 
and exit from depots, line with few traffic that does not justifies larger 
infrastructure costs, and many more peculiar situations. In those circumstances, 
line blocks are installed to prevent LRV to enter a track that is already occupied 
by another vehicle.  
Line blocks might be avoided if the single track is shorter than the 
expected driver sight and the allowable speed is lower than 70/80 km/h. In fact, 
in such circumstance, conventional semaphores or other low-tech priority 
mechanism are considered able to guarantee adequate safety levels.  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Figure 4-13: Left: Bidirectional single track operation in Japan, with track loop. Source: 
http://www.japaneserailwaysociety.com/jrs/members/keith/hkrk/s-0Y120071.jpg [22 April 
20015]. Right: Example of a gantlet track in Portugal. Source: 
http://www.freerails.com/gallery/5615/5615_170706_220000001.jpg [22 April 2015]. 
4.4 Remote LR Operation Control 
The CMC can have knowledge of the status of the LR systems in its 
entirety. In fact, various detection devices, SCADA systems, and various 
components of the signalling systems may also employed to report to the CMC 
the status of various track side equipment, vehicles, and other elements that may 
affect LR operations. That information is not necessarily use in daily dispatching 
operations, as it might happen for conventional rail. In LR contexts, the 
dispatcher, if any, has limited operational responsibilities. In fact, the route are 
set directly by the driver or by automated systems, that are physical located 
either on board or centralized.  
 
==PAGE:90==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: MAIN APPLICATION CONTEXTS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         67 
                 
 
Conversely, the system overview can be used to gather critical 
operational data and performances with the aim of optimization. And, more 
importantly, it is employed in case of contingency of operation due to 
disturbances or obstructions. In fact, in those situations, is crucial to have a live 
status of the system and its component in a centralized place. From there it is 
possible to coordinate all the efforts, optimize, and accelerate recovery actions 
(VDV 2014). 
4.5 Subsystems Related to the Signalling System 
The signalling and control system is made up of a multitude of different 
subsystems, each of them with peculiar characteristics and functionalities. Some 
of them are directly related with safety, some others with operation, and others 
may be used to provide add-on functionalities. The most important subsystems 
that it is possible to find as part of modern LR signalling systems are the 
following (VDV 2014). 
4.5.1 Intermodal Transport Control Systems (ITCS) 
It is a core system in modern LR operation and it is crucial for achieving 
high LR operational performances, but it is not a safety critical system. It quickly 
provides accurate and relevant information on current operating conditions to 
the CMC. In fact, its main functions are the communication monitoring and 
information transmission between the LRVs and the LR control centre allowing a 
high degree of operation efficiency control. Besides, it provides real-time LR 
transit information to passenger and it allows optimized vehicle operations by 
taking into account connection, managing disruptions and timetable deviations, 
and dealing with intersection priority. Furthermore, the LR data gathered by the 
ITCS may be further used for crucial analyses and studies, such as optimisation 
of 
timetabling, 
mathematical 
and 
statistical 
methodologies, 
financial 
controlling, quality assurance, and many more.  
4.5.2 Passenger Safety and Information System (PSIS) 
It is a system that allows communication between the LR control center 
and the passengers in the vehicles and at the stations in case of emergency for 
further help and information. Besides, this system is connected to closed-circuit 
television camera observation with the aim of increase passengers security, 
reduce vandalism and littering, and to collect visual information that can be 
shown in the control room. The PSIS gives additional information to the 
passenger that cannot be displayed within the ITCS purposes; particularly, audio 
==PAGE:91==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: MAIN APPLICATION CONTEXTS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         68 
                 
 
announcements at the stations and in the vehicles in case of disturbed traffic. 
Nevertheless, ITCS and PSIS system have similar functionalities and both 
together increase LR operation reliability. 
4.5.3 Supervisory Control and Data Acquisition (SCADA) 
It is a system that provides control of remote LR-related technical 
equipment and it may be combined with a data acquisition system to obtain 
information about their status (Daneels & Salter 1999). The most common 
equipment connected to SCADA typically are traction power supply, station 
lighting, passenger information displays, ticketing facilities, and turnouts 
heating. 
4.5.4 Automatic Vehicle Location (AVL) 
This non-safety vital system groups all the devices and transmission links 
related with LRV detection and positioning. Particularly, it first automatically 
gathers information about LRV position from the different detector devices and 
positioning system employed (see paragraph 4.4), that can be grouped in the 
system called LRV Detection System (TDS). Next, it communicates that 
information to other systems or elements that need them, such as the LRV driver, 
the intersection priority management system, the control room, and, more 
importantly, to the ITCS. The communication links can be hardwired cables or 
wireless, with the most common wireless systems being radio data transmission 
and GSM. 
4.5.5 Automatic Tram Stop (ATS) 
It is a safety critical system similar to Automatic Train Protection system 
employed in conventional rail. It task is to bring LRV to a complete stop when 
there are dangerous situation ahead if the driver is not responsive. It is an on-
board system that gets track status and occupancy from trackside elements and 
it acts directly on the LRV´s brakes when needed. For instance, if the driver 
overruns a stop sign before an occupied turnout in an interlocked area, the ATS 
brings the LRV to a complete stop before reaching the possible collision point. Its 
application is limited to some interlocked areas and, differently to conventional 
ATP, it does not provide speed supervision.  
 
 
==PAGE:92==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL – ROAD INTERSECTIONS: DESCRIPTION AND LR IPMS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         69 
                 
 
5 LIGHT RAIL – ROAD INTERSECTIONS: 
DESCRIPTION AND LR IPMS  
5.1 Intersections: General Description and Types  
In road and LR contexts, intersections are generally defined as the 
common parts of roads and LR alignments where vehicles belonging to various 
flows diverge, merge or cross with other vehicles’ flows.  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Figure 5-1: Example of a simple intersection with explanation of diverging, merging and crossing 
conflict point. Source: 
http://www.fhwa.dot.gov/publications/research/safety/08071/images/image004.jpg [25 April 
2015] 
In terms of, the higher the angle between the conflicting flows, the higher 
the collision’s severity. Hence, crossing points are more dangerous than both 
merging and diverging points. To mitigate the risk of collision at intersections 
and their severity, a number of strategies have are implemented (explained in 
the following sections) (Canale et al. 2011). 
5.1.1 Time Separation 
Various vehicle flows are given permission to cross the intersection 
during different time windows. The time separation is actuated with fixed signs 
(stop signs) or with signals that vary over time (traffic lights). It is an easy and 
cheap strategy to implement, but it is the least effective, safe, and the ones that 
affect traffic the most. 
5.1.2 Horizontal Separation 
Special or additional road lanes are realized to accommodate different 
traffic flows in such a way that conflict’s points are eliminated and/or collision 
angles are decreased. Examples of this solution are acceleration or diversion 
lanes, turning lanes, and roundabouts. Those solutions are more effective and 
==PAGE:93==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL – ROAD INTERSECTIONS: DESCRIPTION AND LR IPMS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         70 
                 
 
safer than time separation, but they require more space around the intersection.  
5.1.3 Vertical Separation 
Conflict’s points are eliminated by grade separation of some (or all) 
vehicle flows. It is realized by using viaducts, flyovers, underpasses, or tunnels. 
They are generally employed to solve intersections with high traffic volumes, 
where there is the need to give full priority to selected flows, and/or where the 
average vehicle speed is high. It is considered the most effective solution both 
from the traffic flow and from the safety standpoints, but it comes with higher 
costs.  
5.2 Light Rail and Conventional Railway – Road 
Intersections  
In railway terms, at-grade intersections with road are commonly known 
as level crossings. For conventional railways, they are specifically designed to 
mitigate the risk of road vehicle-train crashes and their severity. Due to the high 
kinetic energy of trains, the number of fatalities and the degree of assets damage 
can be very high in case of road vehicle – train crashes. As statistics show, 
accidents at level crossing typically cause around one third of all fatalities in 
railway operation (Evans 2010).  
For rolling stock’s stopping distances are considerably higher than the 
road vehicle (see Chapter 1.1.1), train traffic is always prioritize at level 
crossings, as it is easier for road vehicles than trains to come to a complete stop. 
There are various technical solutions for level crossings, which are 
examined in the following sections.  
5.2.1 Passive Level Crossings 
Passive level crossing is the most basic solution. In this case, only static 
roadside signs (such as Saint Andrew’s crosses, warning, and countdown signals) 
are used to warn road users about the presence of a level crossing. Therefore, no 
signalling equipment are needed. 
The road users are therefore totally responsible for observing the railway 
line and be sure that there are not approaching trains, before passing the level 
crossing. This solution is the least safe, as the risk of accidents is 10 times higher 
than at active level crossings (Åberg 1985). Therefore, this solution is adequate 
only for railroad-pedestrian crossings and low-trafficked intersections where 
both trains and road vehicles are expected to run with speed equal or less to 
about 50 km/h, depending to the country and the application’s context. 
==PAGE:94==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL – ROAD INTERSECTIONS: DESCRIPTION AND LR IPMS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         71 
                 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
5.2.2 Active Level Crossings 
This solution belongs to the sphere of conventional signalling. In this case 
road users are given variable indications according to the trains approaching 
status, which is generally automatically detected. Broadly speaking, this is done 
by using sophisticated railway side signalling equipment connected with road 
barriers and signals, whose purpose is to keep the level crossing clear from road 
vehicles for a long time before the train arrival.  
Therefore, on the one hand active level crossing allows heavy and fast 
trains, with long braking distances, to cross roads with an appropriate safety 
level. On the other side, it heavily disturbs road traffic, since the intersection is 
reserved to train traffic for several minutes (Theeg & Vlasensko 2009). For 
instance, if this solution were to be implemented in downtown areas along a LR 
alignment with LRVs running every 5 minutes in both directions, the crossing 
would be closed for road users the majority of the times. 
 
Figure 5-3: Active level crossing: road signals and barriers. Source: 
http://www.jernbanen.dk/forum/images/uploaded/201110281636544eaada2666b35.jpg [25 
Figure 5-2: Passive level crossing: road vehicles intersection (left, source: 
http://www.ferroviaromanord.altervista.org/il_pericolo_dei_passaggi_a_livello_incustoditi_file/imag
e009.jpg [25 April 2015]) and pedestrian intersection (right, source: 
http://i50.tinypic.com/max7j7.jpg [25 April 2015]) 
==PAGE:95==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL – ROAD INTERSECTIONS: DESCRIPTION AND LR IPMS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         72 
                 
 
April 2015]. 
5.2.3 Grade Separation 
In some circumstances at-grade crossings are difficult or impossible to 
establish due to safety reasons. For instance along high-speed railway lines, in 
freeway-railway intersections, in urban areas with heavy road traffic or where 
the appropriate visibility of the level crossing is obstructed. In such general 
cases, and in many more local situations, intersections are realized with vertical 
separation of the road vehicles and train traffic or by closing the intersection for 
road vehicles and reroute their flows towards safer nearby junctions. This 
solution does not generally require any signalling sysyem. 
5.2.4 LR Level Crossing 
The issue of level crossings have be solved differently in LR applications. 
In fact, the implementation of common railway level crossings is unfeasible in 
such urban and frequent LRV passages context; road traffic would be affected at 
unacceptable levels. But, LRV are light and slow rolling stock that enjoys short 
braking distances and a drive-on-sight regime can be implemented. Besides, due 
to the lower kinetic energy that LRV holds, crashes involving LRV and other road 
users have similar degree of severity compared with other road vehicles. 
Accordingly, under this regime, at-grade LR-road intersections do not 
necessarily need conventional signalling to guarantee an adequate safety levels; 
intersections regulated with traffic light are enough to guarantee safety. 
Conversely, where a drive-on-sight regime is not implementable, conventional 
level crossing or grade separation solutions are required. 
 
Figure 5-4: Example of a pedestrian crossing and a road intersection along a LR alignment. Picture 
==PAGE:96==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL – ROAD INTERSECTIONS: DESCRIPTION AND LR IPMS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         73 
                 
 
by Ranieri Brandani in Florence, LR line T1 [2 April 2015]. 
As a conclusion, from the safety stand point, under normal LR operational 
circumstances LRVs do not necessarily need to be prioritized at LR-road 
intersections, given the capacity to brake within the driver’s sight distance.  
5.3 Light Rail Priority: General Description 
Although LR does not need priority for safety purposes, LR-road 
intersections might affect efficient LRV operation if proper IPMS are not 
implemented. In particular, LR priority at intersections is required to achieve the 
following goals (Li et al. 2014): 
 
Journey-time Savings: Priority measures can be targeted to improve 
journey-time of LRV through a series of junctions. Shorter journey time 
could give competitive advantages to LRVs in comparison to general 
traffic and encourage modal change. If this is the only criteria employed in 
the traffic management, then giving maximum levels of priority to all LR 
vehicles will give the best results. 
 
Regularity and Punctuality: Operational regularity and punctuality are 
among the main factors in passenger perception of LR service 
performance, as these measures affect passenger waiting times at 
stations. Targeting late LRVs or the ones with longer headway times will 
give the best results if this is the only criteria employed in designing the 
priority management policies. 
 
Economic benefit: Economic benefits are another potential objective 
function for priory strategies. This is calculated on the basis of the 
performance of LR and all other traffic at a junction, including the effects 
of passengers waiting at stations.  
So, it is evident that in a LR context IPMS is needed for achieving adequate 
operational efficiency, rather than for safety purposes. 
Nevertheless, it is worth to be mentioned that the need for LR priority 
should not result in severe disturbances, or even obstruction, for road traffic 
(Noyce & Dunn 1997). In fact, those situations may present various risks. First, 
from the safety standpoint, long obstruction times leads to a significant decrease 
in road users’ discipline, which may result in unauthorized crossing manoeuvres 
that increases the chances of LR-road vehicles crashes. Second, if road traffic is 
forecasted to be heavily disturbed by the LR alignment, some key stakeholders – 
such as road users, politicians, and road traffic authorities – may become project 
opponents, resenting a project risk. 
==PAGE:97==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL – ROAD INTERSECTIONS: DESCRIPTION AND LR IPMS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         74 
                 
 
So, LR priority management strategies should be tailored and optimized 
considering the need for achieving high LR operational efficiency while 
preserving adequate level of services for road users.  
Finally, the general effectiveness of intersection priority measures can be 
summarized in the three following facts (Griffin 2005): 
 Priority can save public transport on average 20seconds at each traffic 
signalized junction. 
 Giving public transport priority has little effect on other road users 
 The following list of typical commercial speeds gives an indication of the 
time savings to be expected: 
o Semi-exclusive, No priority: 17km/h 
o Semi-exclusive, With priority: 28km/h 
o Segregated track: 35km/h 
5.3.1 Main Advantages and Disadvantages 
Overall, the main advantages of giving LR intersection priority are (Fehon 
1991): 
 
Faster journey times for LR passengers 
 
More reliable LR services 
 
Cost reductions from a smaller fleet and fewer staff to provide the same 
level of service and other factors, due to higher speed (in general a 7–10% 
cost reduction can be expected from a 10% reduction in journey time due 
to priority) 
 
Safety due to less conflict with other traffic modes 
 
Better use of urban infrastructure, borh LR and roads 
 
A more reliable and faster public transport system is likely to be more 
effective in causing modal shift. 
However, there are a number of disadvantages that need to be 
considered: 
 
Costs both for the initial investment and future maintenance 
 
Impact on other traffic flows, including other forms of public transport 
5.3.2 Technical Solutions for LR Priority: Brief Overview 
To achieve the highest LR operational performances, it would be ideal 
that LRV ride on reserved right of ways along the entire alignment, without any 
intersection with roads. In this case, LRVs are not disturbed by variable road 
traffic conditions. This situation can only be implemented by realizing grade 
separated intersections or by closing every intersection with roads, or a 
==PAGE:98==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL – ROAD INTERSECTIONS: DESCRIPTION AND LR IPMS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         75 
                 
 
combination of the two. But this is a utopian solution that would lead to high 
construction costs and unacceptable impact on road traffic. As a result, metro 
solutions would become preferable, with LR projects generally unfeasible 
(Canale et al. 2011). 
Consequently, to keep LR a feasible and attractive urban transport 
solution, a compromise between construction costs, LR operational efficiency 
and impact on road traffic has to be found. This results in the following general 
intersection designing principles, which have to be adapted considering local 
situations and peculiarities (Brandani 2013):  
 
Intersections are realized with grade separation where the road traffic 
volumes are particularly big and/or where vehicles speed is high 
 
Conventional level crossing are employed where a drive-on-sight regime 
is not feasible, but grade separation is not feasible 
 
When the LR alignment is built, some existing crossings are closed or 
profoundly modified where the intersecting roads are of secondary 
importance 
 
In all other cases, LR-road intersections are realized at-grade with traffic 
light and LR intersection priority; this is the most common solution for LR 
application. 
5.4 Light Rail Priority: At-Grade Crossings with Traffic 
Lights 
At-grade LR-road intersection priority management is a special operational 
strategy that allows LRV to adjust traffic light phases on their route. This strategy 
is meant to prioritize LRV by using a minimum of a junction’s traffic capacity. 
The priority consists in reducing or eliminating delays for LRV at an intersection 
by temporarily altering the traffic signal phases so that an approaching LRV 
receives a proceed indication when it arrives at the intersection (Gardner 2009). 
In designing the priority mechanism with traffic lights, it has to be 
considered that the system should be able to: 
 
Minimization of LRV delays at intersections 
 
Allows both the LR and the traffic light operators maximum amount of 
flexibility  
 
Be safe and easily understandable both by LR drivers and for road users 
 
Be compatible with traffic light systems existing in areas surrounding the 
LR alignment 
 
Be flexible so that it can be implemented, without any substantial 
==PAGE:99==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL – ROAD INTERSECTIONS: DESCRIPTION AND LR IPMS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         76 
                 
 
modifications, in contexts featuring different peculiarities. 
 
 
Figure 5-5: Example of a simple LR-road at-grade intersection with traffic light, it can be seen both 
the road and the LR traffic light. Source: Google Maps [25 April 2015]. 
5.4.1 Active and Passive LR IPMS 
There are two types of LR IPMS: passive and active. Broadly speaking, in 
the first one, the system does not know the LRV position. Active systems, instead, 
get live information of the LRV location. In detail, the two systems have the 
characteristics examined in the following sections.  
5.4.1.1 Passive Priority 
It is the most basic system for LR at-grade intersection priority, which 
does not know the location of LRVs along the alignment. Passive priority systems 
use a predetermined knowledge of LR operations (timetable, running time, 
headway time, etc.) to determine the appropriate priority treatment. In this 
system the signal timings are weighted taking into account the flows containing 
LRV.  
This is a straightforward form of priority at traffic signals which gives 
more green time to the prioritized flows than it would have done otherwise. For 
instance, the LRV departs from a station when the proceed signal is shown and it 
gets proceed signals at every downstream crossing until it reaches the following 
station.  
In this system the LR semaphore phase is part of a fixed sequence and it is 
activated even if there are not any LRVs approaching the intersection. As a result, 
road vehicles may experience unnecessary delays in crossing the intersection. 
==PAGE:100==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL – ROAD INTERSECTIONS: DESCRIPTION AND LR IPMS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         77 
                 
 
Besides, since the phases are fixed, it is impossible for the LR operators to alter 
the semaphore phases according to specific traffic condition and to implement 
flexible traffic optimization policies. On the other hand, it is an inexpensive 
system and easy to implement, since it requires few equipment and 
maintenance. Overall, it is believed that passive priority is not widely used as the 
perceived benefits are modest, at best (Fehon 1991). 
5.4.1.2 Active Priority 
In this case, priority is given by making the traffic signals responsive to 
the arrival of each approaching LR vehicle detected. Active priority can be given 
by implementing different strategies depending on the policy objectives and the 
availability of the infrastructure to support the application. It is a technologically 
advanced system and it has been proven to work efficiently (Fehon 1991). 
The LRV positioning can be transmitted continuously, but with little 
accuracy, from the LRV to the IPMS using GNSS technologies. A higher accuracy 
can be achieved by using local discrete positioning technologies, such as track 
loops or overhead wire tags, combined with on-board odometers. The 
positioning information are then used to implement the most appropriate 
priority strategy based on current LR position and traffic light phasing. 
This priority is implemented by inserting a specific LRV phase within the 
normal road traffic light sequence, when the LRV approaches. Even though 
different IPMS architectures have slightly diverse working principles, overall the 
steps followed by the system are: 
1. The priority request is issued by the LRV few hundred meters before the 
intersection 
2. The IPMS accepts the request and notifies the LRV driver 
3. The current traffic light status is altered to accommodate the LR phase in 
the traffic light phases’ sequence, according to different methods 
4. The traffic light shows the proceed aspect for the LRV and stop aspect to 
crossing road traffic flows 
5. The LRV sends and input to the system when it enters the intersection 
6. The LRV sends and input to the system when it clears the intersection  
7. The traffic lights start again the normal phases’ sequence for road 
vehicles, the stop aspect is shown in the LR traffic light. 
5.4.2 Priority Policies 
Priority policies with active priority systems refer to the various 
strategies that are implemented to alter the quantity and quality of LR priority. 
The quantity of LR priority represents the number of LRVs eligible for priority; 
==PAGE:101==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL – ROAD INTERSECTIONS: DESCRIPTION AND LR IPMS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         78 
                 
 
the most common priority criteria take into account the LRV running status 
according to their timetable. The quality of LR priority refers to the amount of 
priority that they can get, depending on various priority level assigned to 
different road vehicle and LRV. The two most common priority policies are 
analysed in the two subsequent sections (Gardner et al. 2009). 
5.4.2.1 Conditional Priority 
This policy consists of targeting priority to LRV fulfilling pre-defined 
criteria designed to serve particular operational objectives. For instance, one 
common strategy is prioritizing late LRVs only. LRVs that are behind schedule 
receive it, whereas the ones that that are on time or early do not. Conditional 
priority represents a good balance between the needs of road users and LR 
operators. In fact, it can provide a good magnitude of LR travel time savings and 
passenger waiting time savings, while reducing the impact on general road 
traffic.  
A similar policy is employed for LR operated under headway control, i.e. 
giving priority on the basis of their headway. Such a strategy aims to improve 
service regularity rather than punctuality. The latter strategy is to be preferred if 
LR runs have high frequency (e.g. an average headway of 12 minutes or less) 
with passengers tending to arrive randomly at stations.  
From a practical viewpoint it should be noted that this strategy is more 
difficult to implement, due to the requirement that the system has to know the 
timetable and the deviation from it. Therefore, an Automatic Vehicle Location 
system is a prerequisite for obtaining headway and travel time data in real-time. 
5.4.2.2 Maximum Priority 
The most basic policy gives the maximum level of priority to all LRVs and 
it aims to decreases their running time. But, if the headway between LRVs is low 
this strategy may result in unacceptable delays and obstruction for road vehicles; 
representing also a risk of delays for other public transport means, such as 
busses. However, this is one of the simplest strategies to implement, as the only 
information required is the expected arrival time of LRV at the intersection. The 
disadvantages to the road traffic can be reduced by applying maximum priority 
only where or when LRV runs have low or medium frequencies.  
5.4.3 Priority Actualization Levels 
Signal priority can be actualized with two different levels, as following 
described (Gardner et al. 2009). 
==PAGE:102==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL – ROAD INTERSECTIONS: DESCRIPTION AND LR IPMS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         79 
                 
 
5.4.3.1 Partial Priority 
This level consists of widening the green window for LR at a traffic signal 
through one of several potential methods. A wider window reduces the 
probability that the LRV will have to stop. The additional time given to the transit 
vehicle must be taken away from other phases in the fixed cycle length. Partial 
priority also attempts to minimize disruptive effects on road and pedestrian 
traffic. In fact, the amount of extra LR phase length would be limited, and no road 
vehicle phase is skipped under any circumstance. Partial priority treatments are 
often implemented using a form of passive priority.  
5.4.3.2 Full Priority 
It does everything that also partial priority does, but it further allows 
some road vehicle phases to be skipped, or eliminated, to implement LR priority. 
In the pure case of full priority, LR priority requests override all other traffic 
demands at the intersection (usually except the ones from emergency vehicles). 
Full priority provides the best LR operating conditions, but can also lead to 
intolerable road vehicle delays if used indiscriminately. However, full priority 
may be appropriate at some intersections during off-peak periods. 
5.4.4 Re-cap table

Table 5-1: Summary of the various priority types 
5.4.5 Priority Actualization Methods 
Various methods have been idealized to actualize LR priority; the most 
common are (Canale 2011): 
 
Extension: It consists of the widening of the green traffic light phase when 
an approaching LRV is detected towards the end of a traffic light green 
phase. This method is commonly used where the point of detection is 
relatively close to the junction.  
==PAGE:103==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL – ROAD INTERSECTIONS: DESCRIPTION AND LR IPMS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         80 
                 
 
 
Recall: The recall method refers to the recall of the green phase for the LR 
if the traffic light is on a red phase, when an approaching LRV is detected. 
Also this method is used in cases where the point of detection is close to 
the crossing.  
 
Rolling Horizon: These methods use AVL information further upstream 
from the junction and use gradual adaptation of the relevant traffic light 
green phases’ occurrence and duration to match the predicted arrival 
time of the LRV at the various intersections. Those methods have the 
advantage of a less abrupt impact on signal timings, which could 
compromise efficient signal co-ordination. On the other hand, they are 
more dependent on accurate journey time forecasting, that obviously 
deteriorates the further the LRV is from the junction. 
 
Stage Re-ordering: The aforementioned methods described above are 
normally implemented without affecting the normal traffic light phases’ 
structure. An alternative and stronger form of priority is to allocate a 
specific phase to the LRV when detected. This phase is then inserted into 
the sequence at the next opportunity. This means skipping or delaying 
other phases, and may allow a repeated green of a LRV stage, if the LRV is 
detected in the inter-green period immediately after a LRV stage has just 
terminated. This is the most employed method in LR contexts.  
 
Green wave: A special plan is initiated in the UTC system to provide a 
sequence of green signals for the selected priority vehicle. This is often 
implemented for emergency vehicles (particularly ambulances and fire 
appliances) responding to emergency calls but it is not widely spread in 
LR contexts. The long green periods which often result (and long red 
periods to some traffic streams) can be justified by the importance of the 
vehicle and the infrequency of the event; such action can seldom be 
justified for public transport.
