5.4.6 Priority System Architectures 
LR Priority at traffic signals can be achieved with an increasingly large 
number of system architectures and the solutions’ range is really broad. At its 
simplest, local LRV detection systems provide direct LR priority requests, 
through the local traffic signal controller.  
At the other extreme, LRV priority requests can be managed trough a fully 
integrated system with one or two way communication between the LRV on-
board computer and driver, the AVL, the ITCS, the UTC, and the local traffic 
signal controller who manage the traffic light phases. A wide range of variants 
exist between these extremes. Some of these communication links may be one 
way or two-way and some may not be used depending on the way of working of 
the priority system. As a result, the number of design alternatives is relatively 
high, which increases the possibility of finding a system that best suits different 
LR contexts (RARTJV 2015b). 
 
Once the priority policy, method and actualization level are established, 
the priority management system architecture needs to be chosen and designed, 
considering also local circumstances and specific requests. Accordingly, various 
architectures mainly differ according to how the priority requests are sent from 
the LRV to the traffic light controller for its actualization.  
Notwithstanding the differences among the various system architectures, 
the following requirements have to be fulfilled by any architecture to assure 
satisfactory LR and intersection performances: 
 
Reliability: In normal operation conditions, only the request sent by the 
vehicle has to lead to a LR traffic light phase within a predefined time 
lapse, ideally without causing delay to the LRV 
 
Redundancy: In perturbed operations, a fall back system has to be 
implemented to allow the LRV to send priority requests when the 
ordinary priority mechanism is not available. 
A more detailed description of the three most common architectures follows. 
==PAGE:105==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL – ROAD INTERSECTIONS: DESCRIPTION AND LR IPMS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         82 
                 
 
5.4.6.1 Priority Request Via Local Interface 
In this method, the LRV sends the priority request directly to the local 
traffic signal controller (RARTJV 2015b).  
 
Figure 5-6: Sketch of the architecture of the priority management system via local interface  
First, the AVL localizes the LRV along the alignment. It does that with a 
combination of detection devices, the most common for this purpose being track 
loops, odometer combined with GNSS, and door opening systems (see red 
arrows). The location of the vehicle is transmitted to the IBIS. The IBIS then 
calculates the expected arrival time of LRV at the intersection, considering live 
data (such as speed, vehicle weight, etc.) combined with stored infrastructure 
and vehicle data (expected vehicle braking performances, key distances along 
the LR alignment, gradient, etc.). With all those information, the IBIS elaborates 
the priority request according to the pre-set priority policies and methods. After 
that, the LRV sends the intersection priority request directly to the traffic light 
controller. The request can be send either wireless via an on-board radio, or 
wired via the detecting device (see green arrows), or via both of them to ensure 
redundancy. Finally, the traffic light controller implements the priority according 
to the pre-set priority actualization methods by altering the traffic light phases. 
The detector devices B and C then communicate to the traffic signal controller 
when the LRV enters and clears the intersection, so that the LR traffic light phase 
can be terminated as soon as safely possible to favour road vehicles traffic. The 
==PAGE:106==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL – ROAD INTERSECTIONS: DESCRIPTION AND LR IPMS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         83 
                 
 
accuracy of the detection devices B and C need to be higher than the one of the 
devices used for sending the request.  
Besides, the IBIS communicates wireless also with the ITCS and PSIS, 
which control the LR traffic status and provides transit information to the 
passengers via various communication means (passengers’ screens, internet, 
apps, etc.). As an option, the traffic light controller might communicate its status 
to the UTC, whose sole purpose is to optimize vehicle road traffic in other road-
road intersections.  
The main advantage of this architecture is that priority is requested 
directly from the LRV to the traffic light controller. Furthermore, this system can 
be implemented both at isolated signal-controlled junctions as well as in 
coordinated junctions. Besides, this system enjoys high LRV-intersection 
communication reliability and fast priority actualization response time, it is 
relatively easy to implement and, more importantly, allows LR operators to have 
direct control of the LRV priority. On the other hand, it does not allow 
optimization of road traffic on a larger scale and it does require more wayside 
equipment. 
5.4.6.2 Priority Request Via a Fully Centralized Interface 
In this method, the LRV sends the priority request to the local traffic 
signal controller via a centralized interface, usually the Urban Traffic Controller. 
Since this method involves communication between two centres (ITCS and UTC), 
it is also known as Centralised communications (RARTJV 2015b). The sketch of 
this system architecture is depicted in Figure 5-7. 
==PAGE:107==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL – ROAD INTERSECTIONS: DESCRIPTION AND LR IPMS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         84 
                 
 
 
Figure 5-7: Sketch of the architecture of the priority management system via centralized 
interface architecture 
First, as in the previous system, the AVL localizes the LRV along the 
alignment. It does that with a combination of detection devices, the most 
common for this purpose being track loops, odometer combined with GNSS, and 
door opening systems (see red arrows). The location of the vehicle is also 
transmitted to the LRV’s on-board computer, known as IBIS. The IBIS then 
passes the location information to the ITCS, that calculates the expected arrival 
time of LRV at the intersection, considering live data (such as speed, vehicle 
weight, etc.) combined with stored infrastructure and vehicle data (expected 
vehicle braking performances, key distances along the LR alignment, gradient, 
etc.). With all those information, the ITCS elaborates the priority request and 
sends it to the UTC (see green arrow).  
In this architecture it is the UTC that calculates the feasibility of 
implementing changes in the traffic light phases to actualize the LR priority 
request according to predefined policies. The UTC does that by taking into 
account various parameters and indicators, such as the road traffic status and 
other priority requests (from other LRVs, busses, emergency vehicles, etc.), and 
performs an optimization of the traffic flows on a large scale, usually the whole 
municipal area.  
In performing traffic light phases’ calculations and optimization the UTC 
uses either fixed time control policies or traffic responsive ones. In the first one, 
==PAGE:108==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL – ROAD INTERSECTIONS: DESCRIPTION AND LR IPMS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         85 
                 
 
traffic light phases are calculated using historic, measured traffic data to 
generate optimum plans that usually vary by time of day and day of week. In 
other cases, real-time traffic data from strategic detectors in the network are 
used to select the most appropriate traffic light phases plan from a pre-set 
library. Whereas traffic responsive systems rely on traffic detectors to provide 
live data that is used to calculate optimum signal settings in real time. But full 
traffic responsive control carries a significant implementation and maintenance 
cost, so they are not widely spread yet.  
In all cases, once the priority request is processed the information about 
priority actualization is then transmitted from the UTC to the local controller for 
implementation. Next, the local traffic light controller changes the traffic light 
phases to favour LRVs according to the UTC instruction. The detector devices B 
and C in this case communicate to the UTC when the LRV enters and clears the 
intersection, so that the LR traffic light phase can be terminated as soon as safely 
possible to favour road vehicles traffic.  
The main advantage of this system is that it allows a macro road traffic 
optimization. As disadvantages, it is a complex system to implement that has a 
low reliability given by the numerous communication links needed and it has not 
any kind of redundancy. Moreover, it does not allow LR operators to directly 
manage LRV priority, which may result in decreased LR punctuality and average 
speed. Furthermore, this system can be implemented only where a UTC exists.  
5.4.6.3 Priority Request Using a Combination of a Centralised and Local Interface 
This architecture is made up by one of the many possible combinations of 
the two aforementioned architectures and its sketch is shown in Figure 5-8.  
==PAGE:109==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL – ROAD INTERSECTIONS: DESCRIPTION AND LR IPMS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         86 
                 
 
 
Figure 5-8: Sketch of the architecture of the priority management system via UTC and local 
interface architecture 
 
Particularly, in normal working conditions the priority is managed by the 
centralized interface (UTC, see green link (2)). The local interface (see blue 
arrow (3)) is used as a fall-back solution if some of the links between LRV-ITCS-
UTC-traffic light controllers are not functioning. So, thanks to the fall-back mode, 
the failure of some communication link or equipment will not seriously disrupt 
LR operations (RARTJV 2015b).  
As a result, the system has the same communication reliability of the local 
interface solution, it is redundant and it is still possible to realize a macro road 
traffic optimization. It still has the disadvantage that it is a complex system and it 
does not allow LR operators to directly manage LRV priority in normal 
conditions and it is more expensive, since two systems have to be implemented.

5.5 Light Rail Priority Management: Other Solutions 
In some case IMPMS cannot be realized with at-grade traffic lights, 
accordingly some other solutions have to be implemented. 
5.5.1 Grade Separation 
Grade separation is the most effective measure to implement intersection 
priority, but it also is the most expensive. Therefore, generally LR-road 
intersections are realized with grade separation in two cases. The first, in the 
case that vehicle volumes at the intersection are high and an additional traffic 
light, with priority for LRV, would lead to unacceptable level of services for road 
vehicles or would affect LRV operation performances. The other case is where 
the LR alignment crosses highways or roads travelled by road vehicles with high 
speed; in this case the realization of traffic light intersections would lead to 
safety problems (TCRP 2012).  
Grade separation is realized by building tunnels, underpasses (Figure 
5-9), viaducts, or flyovers (Figure 5-10). Usually only LRV passes at a different 
grade (Figure 5-10), but in some cases the LR alignment is put by side to bike or 
pedestrian paths. In some other minor cases a given road traffic flow to be 
prioritized can be put by side to LR alignment (Figure 5-9). However, there are 
not general rules to determine whether should be the roads or the LR alignment 
to change grade; design considerations have to be made locally. It has also to be 
notices that in the design process for grade separated intersections cost and 
environmental impacts play a crucial role. 
 
==PAGE:111==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL – ROAD INTERSECTIONS: DESCRIPTION AND LR IPMS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         88 
                 
 
 
 
Figure 5-10: Example of a LR-road grade separated intersection, where only the LR alignment 
changes grade and crosses a road roundabout in a flyover. Source: 
http://newsletter.comune.fi.it/templates/newsletter.comune.fi.it/img_virtual_cm/2/cm_obj_23422.
JPG[25 April 2015] 
5.5.2 Rerouting of Road Traffic Flows 
The construction of a LR alignment in urban environments presents 
several challenges, one of them definitely being the creation of new urban road 
mobility schemes (plans that set local traffic policies, lane directions and overall 
Figure 5-9: Example of a LR-road grade separated intersection, where the LR alignment (highlighted in 
yellow) and a prioritized road underpass a road roundabout. Satellite and local pictures. Source: Google 
Maps and Google Earth [25 April 2015]. 
==PAGE:112==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL – ROAD INTERSECTIONS: DESCRIPTION AND LR IPMS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         89 
                 
 
mobility goals). In doing that, the following factors may lead to closure or 
modification of some at-grade intersections (Comune di Firenze 2014): 
 
Due to intersection geometrical configurations and visibility issues, it 
might become impossible to keep the intersection open for road traffic 
after the LR alignment has been built 
 
The low traffic flow at intersection with minor roads, that before the LR 
construction where regulated with simple stop or yield signs, might not 
be enough to justify the realization of a traffic light intersection 
 
Once the LR is built the traffic flows are naturally modified, both because 
of modal shift and due to the presence of a new infrastructure that 
normally alters the space available for road users. This lead to changes in 
the volumes of traffic traversing various intersections.  
All those factors and local considerations usually require the definition of 
new mobility schemes on a large area, that may involves multiple municipal 
territories.  In designing them, LR priority considerations have to be taken into 
account and play a crucial role. In fact, a common strategy is to close some LR-
road intersections in order to reroute road vehicle flows towards preselected 
crossing. Those crossing are the ones where is safer to cross the LR alignment 
and/or where LRV priority can be easily implemented with greater impact in 
terms of operational efficiency. For instance, traffic flows that cross the LR 
alignment may be reroute towards grade separated intersections or towards 
roundabout with traffic lights, which are safer due to low collision angles (can be 
seen in Figure 5-11). 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Figure 5-11: Example of rerouting of road vehicles towards a road-LR roundabout. Source: Google 
Maps and Google Earth [25 April 2015] 
==PAGE:113==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL – ROAD INTERSECTIONS: DESCRIPTION AND LR IPMS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         90

6 LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: INTERNATIONAL 
BENCHMARKING 
6.1 Introduction  
The purpose of this chapter is to provide LR performance measures with 
the primary aim of analyse and compare different signalling and priority 
management systems.  
Furthermore, an overview of the geographical distribution of LR 
networks per country is presented. The scope of it is to offer an easy tool for 
assessing the quantitative relevance of countries in terms of LR and its signalling 
systems.  
6.2 Worldwide Distribution of LR networks 
The next Table 6-1 shows the total number of active LR networks per 
country, grouped according to the continent. Even though there are many 
different ways in which LR can be classified, all giving different results, the 
networks that have been considered in the counting are only the ones opened 
after the year 1975 as well as the older networks that have been radically 
modernized after that year. The reason of the choice being that the modern 
concept and principles of LR started developing in the middle of the 1970’s (see 
Chapter 1.5). Therefore older systems are expected to have system features 
dissimilar from the modern LR concepts. 
This counting has been done in order to show where knowledge and 
expertise about LR and its signalling systems is most likely to be found. 
Moreover, it is expected that the greatest the amount and concentration of LR in 
a country is, the higher the possibility to find there relevant LR regulations 
and/or standards. Those are very important information in various project 
phases, when specific LR knowledge, expertize, standards, and regulations are 
key inputs and tools that boost the likelihood of project success.

USA having the greatest number of networks in the respective continents. In

6.3 Quantitative Performance Benchmarking 
This chapter features a quantitative performance analysis of different LR 
networks in terms of intersection priority management and signalling systems.  
The LR networks analysed have been chosen considering the quantity and 
quality of available data, as well as the geographical distribution, and the right-
of-way variety. It has to be mentioned that the most critical data to get, therefore 
the most constraining parameter in the choice of the networks, was about the 
IPMS architecture and its working principles.  
 For every LR system analysed a table holding a number of signalling-
related data and relevant parameters was created. The meaning of the data and 
an explanation of their relevance is given in an informative table shown in 
paragraph 6.3.1. Whereas the actual line data are presented in the appendix 
(page 146) in order to ease the readability of the present document. However, a 
summarizing table of the most relevant line parameters in terms of signalling is 
presented in Chapter 6.3.2. The actual performance analysis as well as a 
comparison of signalling and priority management systems data, with the 
relevant conclusions, is carried out in Chapter 6.3.3. 
6.3.1 Explanatory Table 
The following table holds the explanation of the parameters used in the 
analysis, as well as their relevance for the current study.  
General Network and Line Data 
Number of lines 
Total number of lines within the LR network. It gives a general idea about 
the network size and relevance.  
Total network 
length 
Total network length in kilometres. It gives a more detailed quantitative 
indication of the network size and relevance. 
Line analysed 
Which line of the network has been chosen for the analysis. The choice 
has been made considering the available data and the relevance of the 
line within the network, as pointed out in Chapter Error! Reference 
source not found.. 
Opening year 
In which year the line was opened. It is important to give a first 
impression about the presumable technological level of the line and its 
infrastructure, the intersection priority management, and signalling 
solutions.  
Length 
Total line length in kilometre; it is used to calculate the average line 
speed. 
==PAGE:117==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: INTERNATIONAL BENCHMARKING 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         94 
                 
 
Running time 
The time spent by the LRV to cover the terminal-to-terminal distance in 
normal operation, according to the public timetable. It is used to calculate 
the average line speed. 
Average speed 
Average running speed in normal operation, calculated as line length 
divided by running time. It is a very important LR performance 
parameter and benchmark data. In fact, the expected patronage, 
operational costs, and a number of other significant LR performances 
depend to the average line speed (Tirachini et al. 2010). 
Number of stations 
Total number of stations along the analysed line, including the terminals. 
It is used to calculate the average distance between stations. 
Average distance 
between stations 
The average length between two consecutives stations where LRV come 
to a full stop. It is calculated as line length divided by the total number of 
stations. It is a key factor as it may affect the average LR line speed and 
patronage (Kuby et al. 2004). 
Gini coefficient for 
stations 
The Gini coefficient is a statistical dimensionless inequality measure of a 
given distribution; it gives indication about the concentration (or 
spreading) of the considered statistical samples. It is largely used in 
economics as an income inequality indicator (Dorfman 1979). In the 
present section, the Gini coefficient is used to assess the variance of the 
actual distance between the stations in the analysed lines. In general, the 
higher the number is, the more unequal the distances between the 
stations along the line are. In the case of LR stations distribution, the Gini 
coefficient goes from 0%, representing a case with all stations equally 
spaced, up to 100%, representing the (physically impossible) case with 
all stations located in one single point. It is an important factor in the 
present analysis as it may affect the average speed and other operational 
performances. 
Vehicle 
manufacturer-
model 
The name of the company that manufactured the LRV used in the 
considered line and the vehicle’s commercial name. It is not expected to 
be an aspect that affects signalling performances, but it is an important 
general information. 
Number of driving 
cab(s) 
Whether the LRV is single or double ended. It has signalling implications 
for the on-board installation. This factor is mainly related with the 
terminals type and depot layout (see Chapter 4.1.1 and Chapter 4.2). 
Yearly patronage 
Total number of passengers using the line per year. If the data for the line 
is not available, the yearly network patronage is used. It is an important 
information on how the system is perceived and used by the population, 
but it has not signalling implications. 
Network map and indication of the analysed line. It is a powerful visual information giving an 
overview of the system, its peculiarities, and size. 
==PAGE:118==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: INTERNATIONAL BENCHMARKING 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         95 
                 
 
Right-of-way Composition 
Mixed traffic 
The length and percentage of the line in which LRV runs mixed with road 
vehicles (see 2.3.1.1). This parameter may affect the average speed and 
other key operational LR performances (Boscia & Wilkins 1985), reason 
why it is a key factor to analyse in the present study.  
Semi-exclusive 
The length and percentage of the line in which LRV runs in a partially 
segregated alignment, but there are at-grade intersections with roads. 
The typical situation is where the LR alignment is built within or next to 
existing roads; but it is separated from them with curbs, apart at 
intersections (see 2.3.1.2). This parameter may affect the average speed 
and other key operational LR performances (Boscia & Wilkins 1985), 
reason why it is a key factor to analyse in the present study. 
Exclusive 
The length and percentage of the line in which LRV runs in a totally 
segregated alignment. The typical situation is where the LR alignment is 
totally separated from road traffic and other potential obstructions by 
means of fences, high curbs, or similar devices (see 2.3.1.3). This 
parameter may affect the average speed and other key operational LR 
performances (Boscia & Wilkins 1985), reason why it is a key factor to 
analyse in the present study. 
==PAGE:119==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: INTERNATIONAL BENCHMARKING 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         96 
                 
 
Degree of 
exclusiveness 
The degree of exclusives is a parameter that has been created specifically 
for this study in order to ease the comparison between different LR right-
of-way compositions. In fact, since LR alignments potentially have 3 
different compositions, the analysis of the right-of-way data is easier if 
done with one parameter only, that include all the three components. In 
other words, it is interesting to label different LR lines with an unique 
measure holding information about their level of separation from road 
traffic. For instance, considering only the percentage of the line in 
exclusive right-of-way would not have covered the entire spectrum of 
possibilities. Accordingly, the degree of exclusiveness has been created as 
a weighted average of the right-of-way compositions; with the higher 
weight (3) given to exclusive portions, the smallest to mixed traffic (1), 
and an intermediate weight (2) to semi-exclusive portion of track. The 
philosophy behind it is to give a superior importance to alignments 
having greater extension of segregated right-of-way, and less importance 
to the ones with non-segregated traffic. Besides, the potential variation 
range of the degree of exclusiveness has been set from 0 to 100%. So, a 
LR alignment with mixed traffic only has a degree of exclusiveness of 0%, 
a totally semi-exclusive alignment of 50%, and a fully exclusive alignment 
has a degree of exclusiveness of 100%. 
Therefore, considering all the aforementioned inputs and settings, the 
formula that has been created to calculate the degree of exclusiveness is: 
                        [ ]    
 [          
 
    
 ] 
Where: - M = Percentage of the LR alignment with mixed traffic 
- SE = Percentage of the LR alignment with semi-exclusive traffic 
 
- E = Percentage of the LR alignment with exclusive traffic 
 
It has to be noticed that different formulae has been tried and the 
parameters’ weight changed to see how the results, and their 
significance, vary. However, the formula above is considered to be the 
most relevant for the purpose of this analysis, as shown and explained in 
detail in the next paragraph 6.3.3.7. 
Signalling-related Data 
Maximum 
allowable speed 
The top speed allowed on the analysed line. This parameter is key in 
determining the amount of signalling equipment, their functionalities, 
and the running regime: drive-on-sight below 70 km/h and conventional 
signalling above it (Hellewell 1983). 
Minimum 
headway 
The headway time used in peak hours. It may affect the choice of the 
signalling solution in heavily trafficked portions. Besides, it can be used 
as an overall LR performance indicator.  
Portion with single 
track 
The length and percentage of the line in which LRV runs on single track. 
This parameter heavily affects the running regime as well as the choice 
and the degree of the signalling solutions that need to be installed for 
safety purposes (see Section 4).

Portion with 
gauntlet track 
The length and percentage of the line in which LRV runs on gauntlet 
track. This parameter heavily affects the running regime as well as the 
choice and the degree of the signalling solutions that need to be installed 
for safety purposes (see Section 4). 
Tunnel sections 
The length and percentage of the line in which LRV runs on tunnel. This 
parameter may affect the running regime as well as the choice and the 
degree of the signalling solutions that need to be installed for safety 
purposes (see Section 4). 
Terminals type 
The type and layout of the terminals. It is an important factor to be taken 
into account in the signalling design and in determining the number of 
driving cabs (see Section 4). 
Number of 
turnouts 
The total number of turnouts along the line (excluded the ones in the 
terminals, as they are catalogued in the previous point). They are a key 
factor to be taken into account in the design of the LR signalling system 
(see Section 4). 
Turnouts per 
kilometre 
It is the total number of turnout along the alignment divided by the line 
length. It is an important parameter for checking the influence of the 
turnouts on the LR performances. 
==PAGE:121==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: INTERNATIONAL BENCHMARKING 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         98 
                 
 
Degree of 
Conventional 
Signalling 
In order to ease the comparison of LR signalling-related parameters, a 
unique coefficient grouping all of them, called degree of conventional 
signalling, has been created specifically for this study. The logic 
behind it is the same as the one followed in the case of the degree of 
exclusiveness. Meaning, that different parameters are grouped into a 
single one for easing their comparison. The coefficient of conventional 
signalling is calculated taking into account the main parameters, 
factors, and equipment that may inhibit the drive-on-sight regime. In 
particular, the circumstances that have been considered are where 
there are single track portions with bidirectional operations, sections 
of track within tunnels, exclusive alignments as well as and the total 
number of turnouts (see Chapter 4). The coefficient is a weighted 
average and the philosophy of it is to give different importance to 
diverse factors, in order to better model the complexity of the needed 
signalling equipment. Thus, bidirectional single track has a great 
impact on LR operations and the magnitude of signalling equipment 
needed is also higher, therefore they are assigned the highest weight. 
Tunnel sections also have impact on the signalling, but less than single 
track operation; therefore they have assigned a lower weight. Fully 
exclusive alignments may require additional signalling equipment, 
both for securing level crossings, if any, and for detection of external 
objects; therefore they have also assigned a medium weight.  On the 
other hand, turnouts generally have a minor impact on operations and 
they have assigned the lowest weight. The idea was to assign a degree 
of conventional signalling of 100% if the line is entirely a single 
exclusive track, it is built in a tunnel for the 100% of its length, and it 
has 62 turnouts. Accordingly, the degree of conventional signalling is 
calculated with the following formula: 
    [ ]  (
                
  
 
) 
Where: - ST = Percentage of the line with bidirectional Single Track 
operation 
- Tu = Percentage of the line within tunnel 
- E = Percentage of exclusive alignment 
- TO = The total number of turnouts along the line. This number is 
divided by 62 as the maximum number of turnouts per line in the 
analysed cities. 
 
It has to be noticed that different formulae has been tried and the 
parameters’ weight changed to see how the results, and their 
significance, vary. However, the formula above is considered to be the 
most relevant for the purpose of this analysis, as shown and explained 
in detail in the next paragraph 6.3.3.8. 
==PAGE:122==

By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         99 
                 
 
Intersection Priority Data 
Number of 
intersections with 
traffic light (semi-
exclusive) 
The total number of LR-road intersections with traffic light in the semi-
exclusive portion. It is an important factor to be taken into account in the 
priority design as it may affect the LR average speed and other system 
performances (see 5.3). The considered intersections are only then ones 
in the semi-exclusive part as exclusive alignment does not have 
intersections with traffic light by definition; and in the mixed right-of-
way the priority mechanism, if any, may differ from the one used in semi-
exclusive portion of track. 
Average distance 
between 
intersections 
(semi-exclusive) 
The average length between two consecutives intersections, calculated as 
the length of the semi-exclusive right-of-way divided by number of 
station. It is important as it may affect the average speed and other 
performance parameters. 
Gini coefficient for 
intersection (semi-
exclusive) 
In this case the Gini coefficient, as described previously, is used to assess 
the variance of the actual distance between intersections in the semi-
exclusive portion. In the case of LR intersections distribution, the 
coefficient goes from 0%, representing a case with all intersections 
equally spaced, up to 100%, representing the (physically impossible) 
case with all intersections located in one single point. In general, the 
higher the number is, the more unequal the distances between the 
intersections are. It is an important factor in the present analysis as it 
may affect the average speed and other operational performances. 
Specific LR traffic 
signals 
If the system has specific LR signals at traffic light. It is an important 
specification for the intersection priority request design. 
Direct priority 
request 
If this architecture is implemented, the LRV sends the intersection 
priority request directly to the road traffic signal controller. It is a key 
factor for the design of the intersection priority mechanism and its 
expected performances (see 5.4.6.1). 
Priority request 
via UTC 
If this architecture is implemented, the LRV sends the intersection 
priority request to the road traffic signal controller via the UTC. It is a key 
factor for the design of the intersection priority mechanism and its 
expected performances (see 5.4.6.2). 
Uncontrolled 
intersection 
Whether there are some at-grade intersections that are not controlled 
with traffic lights. This parameter may affect safety and LR performance 
(Wu 2007). 
At-grade priority 
architecture 
Detailed description of the LR intersection priority management 
architecture employed in the analysed city. 
Conventional level 
crossings 
If there are some conventional level crossings along the line. This factor 
affects the design of the LR signalling system (Cataldi & Rajee 2001) 
Grade separation 
If there are some LR-road intersections with grade separation along the 
line. This factor affects the LR infrastructure design, but the trigger for the 
construction of grade-separated intersections usually comes from traffic 
and signalling considerations (see 5.5.1).

(Tirachini et al. 2010). However, other data and parameters are used in the

comparison and analyses. This table summarizes the data hold in Table 7-3 and in Table A-1 though

==PAGE:124==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: INTERNATIONAL BENCHMARKING 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         101 
                 
 
comparisons and analyses. A detailed description of every specific analysis is 
given in the following subsections. 
The majority of the analyses presented are done as a regression analysis. 
This is one of the most important statistical methodologies employed in research 
and practical applications (Sykes 1993). The regression analysis technique is 
used with the aim of estimating the existence and the type of the relationship 
between a dependent and an independent variable. The trend of the relationship 
is represented by a function, with the dependent variable presented as a function 
of the independent one. The strength of the relationship is given by the 
coefficient of variation (R2); it indicates how closely the regression analysis’s 
trend fits the actual data. In other words, the R2 is a measure of the extent to 
which the total variation of the dependent variable is explained by the regression 
analysis. This coefficient can assume values between 0 and 1; the closer R2 get to 
1, the better the linear regression explains the relationship between the 
variation of the dependent variable and the correspondent variation of the 
independent one. Another important factor to be checked is the significance of 
the regression (F), that tells if the results from the regression analysis are 
statistically significant or, in other words, if the trend is reliable from the 
statistical standpoint. If the significance F is less than 0,05, the regression 
analysis is statistically significant, otherwise it is not. It has to be mentioned that 
the statistical significance F is only valid for linear regression analyses. The type 
of regression, whether linear or logarithmic, has been chosen according to the 
shape of the points in the graph, in order to fit the data best. 
The data of the Copenhagen Ring 3 LR project are not used for the 
regression analyses. However, the line is displayed on the graphs (with red 
squared box) for benchmarking purposes. The actual and detailed data can be 
found in Chapter 7.4.1. 
6.3.3.1 Opening Year - Speed 
The purpose of this analysis is to check whether there is a relationship 
between the opening years of the LR lines and their average speed. Since 
conventional railways have been significantly increasing their average 
operational speed in the last 50 years (Fröidh 2014) (with high speed rail 
definitely playing a crucial role in that), the idea of this analysis is to check if LR 
followed the same trend. In other words, it is interesting to assess if more 
modern LR systems, that are supposed to be equipped with more modern 
signalling technologies, have higher average speed, as it has happened with 
conventional railways line. Moreover, it is interesting to see if the trend can 
==PAGE:125==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: INTERNATIONAL BENCHMARKING 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         102 
                 
 
explain the future development of the LR average speed for newly opened lines.  
 
Figure 6-1: Comparison and linear regression analysis of the relationship between average speed 
and opening year of the analysed LR lines. The blue dots are the networks considered in the 
regression analysis; the red box refers to Copenhagen Ring 3 LR project. 
As is can be inferred from the plot and the regression analysis, the 
relationship between opening year and average line speed is weak (R2 = 0,018) 
and not statistically significant (F>>0,05). So, it seems that the opening year, and 
more modern LR signalling technologies, does not have a significant effect on the 
average speed as conversely has happened with conventional railways. 
Therefore, knowing the opening year of a given LR line is not enough to estimate 
its expected operational average speed. 
6.3.3.2 Average Station Distance - Speed 
This analysis is carried out with the aim of assessing how the average 
distance between stations affects the LR average speed. In fact, Cervero claims 
that the closer LR stations are, the lower the operational speed results (Cervero 
1984). This can be explained by the fact that stations are certainly locations 
where the LRV have to come to a complete stop. Therefore, the more stops there 
are in a given distance, the lower the speed results.  
==PAGE:126==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: INTERNATIONAL BENCHMARKING 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         103 
                 
 
 
Figure 6-2: Comparison and logarithmic regression analysis of the relationship between average 
speed and average station distances of the analysed LR lines. The blue dots are the networks 
considered in the regression analysis; the red box refers to Copenhagen Ring 3 LR project. 
The relationship shows a strong fit with the proposed logarithmic 
regression (R2 = 0,8462) and the claim from Cervero is therefore confirmed by 
the analysed LR networks. The regression that best fit those data is a logarithmic 
one, however the fit has been proven to be good also with linear regression.  
However, in order to make this analysis even stronger, it is interesting to 
see what happens if the three networks with average speed higher than 29,9 
km/h are removed from the regression. Two of the outliers are the network of 
Manchester and Los Angeles-Long Beach. Those are the only two lines realized, 
in the suburban part, on former conventional railways which have, by nature, 
different features than newly built urban LR lines. The other is the line of 
Amsterdam, which has the unique peculiarity of being realized on 100 % 
exclusive alignment. 
==PAGE:127==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: INTERNATIONAL BENCHMARKING 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         104 
                 
 
 
Figure 6-3: Comparison and logarithmic regression analysis of the relationship between average 
speed and average station distances of the analysed LR lines, excluded three outliers (Amsterdam, 
Manchester, and Los Angeles – Long Beach). The blue dots are the networks considered in the 
regression analysis; the red box refers to Copenhagen Ring 3 LR project. 
When the three outliers are removed, the regression analysis give results 
that fit better the given data (R2 = 0,877). However, the coefficient of regression 
does not substantially increase when the three points are removed. Accordingly, 
it can be concluded that, both with and without the outliers, the average station 
distance has a statistical relationship with the average LR line speed. 
In both cases the trends have an upwards slope, which of course has a 
constraint given by the maximum allowable speed on the line. 
6.3.3.3 Station Distribution - Speed 
Gu claims that equally distributed stations, combined with signalling and 
running supervision technologies, lead to aided LR driving and more energy-
efficient operations (Gu et al. 2014). This is due to less and more even 
acceleration/braking cycles and computer-assisted operations. In this regard, it 
is interesting to check whether the station distribution inequality has also an 
impact on average LR speed. The hypothesis is that the stations distribution 
inequalities (expressed by the Gini coefficient) does not significantly affect the 
LR speed. This is because it does not seem that any parameter related with 
station distribution inequalities may impact the average speed; which is, 
conversely, significantly impacted by the average distance between stations. 
==PAGE:128==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: INTERNATIONAL BENCHMARKING 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         105 
                 
 
 
Figure 6-4: Comparison and linear regression analysis of the relationship between average speed 
and the Gini coefficient for the station distribution of the analysed LR lines. The blue dots are the 
networks considered in the regression analysis; the red box refers to Copenhagen Ring 3 LR project. 
The linear regression does not show any relationship between the 
average speed and the station distribution inequality (Significance F = 0,5021 >> 
0,05) and the proposed trend does not fit the data (R2 of 0,0667).  
However, since there seems to be two possible outliers (Florence and 
Manchester with Gini coefficient higher than 60 %), another regression is carried 
out without those points (Figure 6-5). 
 
Figure 6-5: Comparison and linear regression analysis of the relationship between average speed 
and the Gini coefficient for the station distribution of the analysed LR lines, excluded two outliers 
(Florence and Manchester). The blue dots are the networks considered in the regression analysis; 
the red box refers. 
==PAGE:129==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: INTERNATIONAL BENCHMARKING 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         106 
                 
 
Even though the second regression fits better the data than the out with 
the outliers (R2 = 0,5017), it does not have statistical significance (F > 0,05). 
Therefore, it is concluded that the station distribution inequalities does 
not affect the average LR speed. Thus, signalling and detection technologies can 
be used for implementing energy-efficient driving without affecting speed, a very 
important operational performance. 
6.3.3.4 Average Intersection Distance - Speed 
The aim of this comparison is to assess how the average distance between 
LR-road intersections with traffic lights affects the LR average speed. Since all 
the examined cities have a IPMS, it is expected that the average intersection 
distance does not affect the LR speed, as thoroughly explained in Chapter 5. In 
fact, when the LRV approaches the intersection it gets the green line, in the 
majority of the case. Thus it does not need to brake and the LR operational speed 
should not negatively affected by the intersections. 
It has to be mentioned that the LR of Amsterdam is not included in the 
current analysis, as it does not have any intersection with traffic light. 
 
Figure 6-6: Comparison and linear regression analysis of the relationship between average speed 
and average intersection distances of the analysed LR lines (Amsterdam excluded). The blue dots 
are the networks considered in the regression analysis; the red box refers to Copenhagen Ring 3 LR 
project. 
 
As it was hypothesized, the average distance between intersections does 
not affects the LR speed. The regression it is not statically significant 
(Significance F = 0,6524 >> 0,05) and, moreover, the trend does not fit the data 
(R2 = 0,0361). Therefore, it can be concluded that the intersection priority 
==PAGE:130==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: INTERNATIONAL BENCHMARKING 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         107 
                 
 
management systems, installed in all the analysed cities, fulfil their primary goal 
of not slowing down the LRV at road intersections.  
6.3.3.5 Intersections Distribution - Speed 
As done with the station distribution, it is interesting to check whether 
the distribution of the intersections along the alignment has an impact on the 
average speed. It is expected that the intersection inequalities does not affect the 
average LR speed. In fact, as explained in the previous chapter 6.3.3.4, since the 
intersection priority management is effective in not slowing down the LRV at 
crossings, the intersection distribution should not affect the speed either.  
Also in this case, the LR of Amsterdam is not included in the analysis. 
 
Figure 6-7: Comparison and linear regression analysis of the relationship between average speed 
and the Gini coefficient for the intersection distribution of the analysed LR lines (Amsterdam 
excluded). The blue dots are the networks considered in the regression analysis; the red box refers 
to Copenhagen Ring 3 LR project. 
As imagined, the Gini coefficient for intersection distribution does not 
affect the LR speed. In fact, the regression it is not statically significant 
(Significance F = 0,3812 >> 0,05) and, moreover, the trend does not fit the data 
(R2 = 0,1295).  
6.3.3.6 Type of Intersection Priority Management Architecture - Speed 
The performance comparison between intersection priority management 
architecture and average line speed cannot be carried out as a regression 
analysis since the priority management type is not a quantitative data. However, 
by looking at the data in the next Table 6-4, it does not seem that there is a 
correlation between the architecture types and the line speed. In fact, the LR 
==PAGE:131==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: INTERNATIONAL BENCHMARKING 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         108 
                 
 
networks where the intersection priority request is sent to the traffic light via 
the UTC have comparable performances than the networks with direct priority 
request. For instance, the line of Gold Coast (Direct request and via UTC) and 
Porto (Direct request only) have almost the same average speed, namely 23,6 
km/h and 23,7 km/h respectively. Also, it is highlighted how, among the 
analysed lines, the priority request is never send via UTC only.  
 
Table 6-4: Comparison of various LR intersection priority management architectures and average 
line speed 
This result, together with the ones from the two previous chapters 6.3.3.4 
and 6.3.3.5, gives a strong evidence about the fact that intersection priority 
management systems are effective in preventing LRV from stop at every junction 
– despite their functional architectures. 
6.3.3.7 Right-of-Way Composition - Speed 
The purpose of this comparison is to check which kind of relationship, if 
any, there is between the right-of-way composition and the average speed in the 
analysed LR lines. It is argued that there is a strong relationship between those 
two parameters: the higher the degree of exclusiveness, the average higher the 
speed, resulting also in higher patronage levels (Ben-Akiva and Morikawa 2002).  
This analysis is particularly relevant for the signalling systems, as semi-
exclusive and exclusive right-of ways usually require a higher degree of 
complexity regarding signalling technologies and IPMS. So, if the hypothesis by 
Ben-Akiva and Morikawa is found to be true, it is worth to invest more money in 
signalling technologies that support more exclusive alignments, in order to 
achieve higher operational speed.  
The comparison between right-of-way composition and average LR speed 
is done using the so-called “degree of exclusives”, as explained in Chapter 6.3.1.

Figure 6-8: Comparison and linear regression analysis of the relationship between average speed 
and the degree of exclusiveness of the analysed LR lines. The blue dots are the networks considered 
in the regression analysis; the red box refers to Copenhagen Ring 3 LR project. 
The linear regression analysis proves that there is a strong dependency 
between the degree of exclusiveness and the average speed. In fact, as Ben-Akiva 
and Morikawa argue, the higher the separation between unpredictable road 
traffic and LR alignment, the lower the disturbances for LR optimal run and, 
therefore, the higher the average speed. In terms of signalling, this means that 
intersection priority management for semi-exclusive alignments and more 
advanced LR signalling technologies for exclusive alignment are key to achieve 
higher operational performances. 
Since the formula of the degree of exclusiveness has been created for this 
study, the results of the analysis may change if the formula was different. So, it 
has been tested what happens if the formula for the calculation of the degree of 
exclusiveness, presented in Chapter 6.3.1, was different. In the following figure 
two different formulae have been tested: on the left the degree of exclusiveness 
is calculated as the sum of the percentage of semi-exclusive and exclusive 
alignment; on the right it is the percentage of exclusive alignment only.  
==PAGE:133==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: INTERNATIONAL BENCHMARKING 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         110 
                 
 
 
Figure 6-9: Comparison and linear regression analysis of the relationship between average speed 
and the degree of exclusiveness of the analysed LR lines, calculated with two different formulae. On 
the left, calculated as the sum of the percentage of semi-exclusive and exclusive alignment; on the 
right it simply is the percentage of exclusive alignment.  
It can be see how the formula used on left example in Figure 6-9 gives 
results that fit significantly less than the formula presented in Chapter 6.3.1;  the 
statistical significance F is also lower. The degree of exclusiveness calculated 
with the percentage of fully exclusive alignment (right graph) gives comparable, 
but slightly worse, statistical results than the chosen formula. Nevertheless, the 
formula presented in Chapter 6.3.1 result to be the one giving results that best fit 
the LR networks data, among the three analysed alternative formulae. Besides, 
also semi-exclusive alignments, that require signalling equipment, are included 
into a formulation that takes into account all the possible right-of-way 
configurations.   
6.3.3.8 Degree of Conventional Signalling - Speed 
The assessment of the effects of conventional signalling principles and 
equipment on average LR line speed is carried out in this chapter. In particular, 
all those aspects and circumstances that limit or prevent the establishment of 
drive-on-sight running regime and require conventional signalling equipment, 
apart from higher operational speed, are analysed. The aspect of higher 
operational speed is considered in the next chapter. 
The degree of conventional signalling (see Chapter 6.3.1) is used for the 
assessment. The hypothesis is that the degree of conventional signalling and the 
average LR speed are not related, as the signalling equipment are installed 
mainly for safety reasons. In other terms, the signalling devices prevent the LRV 
to slow down in those circumstances where it would be otherwise unsafety to 
proceed with normal speed. For instance, in tunnels, trough turnouts, and in 
single track the signalling system allow LRV to proceed in a safe manner without 
operationally unacceptable slowing down. Thus, the average LR speed should 
not be affected if proper signalling technologies are in place. This principle is 
==PAGE:134==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: INTERNATIONAL BENCHMARKING 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         111 
                 
 
different in the part of the track where the maximum speed is higher; in those 
cases is the signalling system that allows reaching higher speed. This is the 
reason why maximum allowable speed is compared with average speed in a 
different chapter.  
 
Figure 6-10: Comparison and linear regression analysis of the relationship between average speed 
and degree of conventional signalling of the analysed LR lines. The blue dots are the networks 
considered in the regression analysis; the red box refers to Copenhagen Ring 3 LR project. 
As expected, both the fit of the regression (R2 = 0,4302) and its statistical 
significance (F = 0,0551 > 0,05) confirm that signalling equipment does not have 
effect on the average LR speed as they allow LRV to not slow significantly down 
in potentially dangerous situations.  
Since the formula of the degree of conventional signalling has been 
created for this study, the results of the analysis may change if the formula was 
different. So, it has been checked what happens if the formula for the calculation 
of the degree of conventional signalling, presented in Chapter 6.3.1, was 
different. In the following two figures different formulae have been tested: on the 
left the degree of conventional signalling is calculated as non-weighted average 
of the aforementioned parameters (the same used in the chosen formula); on the 
right, the weight has been changed giving more importance to single track and 
tunnel portion of the alignment; the tested formula is: 
   [ ]  (
                
  
  
) 
==PAGE:135==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: INTERNATIONAL BENCHMARKING 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         112 
                 
 
 
Figure 6-11: Comparison and linear regression analysis of the relationship between average speed 
and the degree of conventional signalling of the analysed LR lines, calculated with two different 
formulae. On the left, calculated as  non-weighted average of the aforementioned parameters; on the 
right the weighted has been changed giving more importance to single track and tunnel portion of 
the alignment. 
It can be see how both the formulae used in Figure 6-11 gives results that 
fit significantly less than the formula presented in Chapter 6.3.1, both in terms of 
significance F and R2. Thus, considering the three presented alternatives, the 
chosen formula it is the one giving results that best fit the LR networks data. 
6.3.3.9 Degree of Conventional Signalling – Minimum Headway 
Another interesting comparison to do is to see whether there is a 
relationship between the degree of conventional signalling and the minimum 
headway time. It is expected that in those circumstances where signalling 
systems need to be installed, the train spacing needs to be higher due to the 
functionalities of the most common detection and interlocking equipment. In 
fact, signalling technologies can represent a strong limitation in achieving 
shorter headway times (Landex 2008). Accordingly, the aim of the regression is  
of checking whether the same situation happens in LR. 
==PAGE:136==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: INTERNATIONAL BENCHMARKING 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         113 
                 
 
 
Figure 6-12: Comparison and linear regression analysis of the relationship between minimum 
headway time and degree of conventional signalling of the analysed LR lines. The blue dots are the 
networks considered in the regression analysis; the red box refers to Copenhagen Ring 3 LR project. 
This linear regression, differently from expected, shows neither 
statistically relevant results (Significance F = 0,3567 >> 0,05) nor a strong fit 
with the data (R2 = 0,1221). But there is a clear outlier, namely the LR line of 
Alicante. It has a headway time of 30 minutes that is significantly higher than the 
range of headway times of all the other lines (between circa 3 and 13 minutes). 
Thus, another analysis is carried out without that point, to see how the result 
changes. 
==PAGE:137==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: INTERNATIONAL BENCHMARKING 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         114 
                 
 
 
Figure 6-13: Comparison and linear regression analysis of the relationship between minimum 
headway time and the degree of conventional signalling of the analysed LR lines, excluded one 
outlier (Alicante). The blue dots are the networks considered in the regression analysis; the red box 
refers to Copenhagen Ring 3 LR project. 
Although the regression without the outlier show a better fit than the 
previous one, the R2 is still low (0,285) and the statistical significance F > 0,05. 
So, conversely from what happens for conventional rail, the signalling systems 
do not have effect on the setting of the minimum headway time in LR context. 
This may be due to the fact that the minimum headway time in LR operations are 
possibly set according to the passenger demand and are not constrained by the 
signalling technological solutions, as it happens in capacity-constrained 
conventional railways (such as the S-train lines in the central part of 
Copenhagen). So, it might be that the need train spacing required by the 
signalling functionalities, is lower than the one set in order to efficiently fulfil the 
passenger demand. However, this assumption has to be confirmed by more 
detailed analyses that go beyond the scope of the present thesis. 
6.3.3.10 Degree of Conventional Signalling – Opening Year 
Following the same principle as in Chapter 6.3.3.1, it is interesting to 
check whether the degree of conventional signalling has been evolving overtime. 
The hypothesis is that opening year and signalling are not related. This is 
because LR signalling systems have always been needed to guarantee adequate 
safety levels in given conditions (single tracks, tunnels, etc.), which, supposedly, 
have not changed overtime. Moreover, the technological improvements that 
==PAGE:138==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: INTERNATIONAL BENCHMARKING 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         115 
                 
 
signalling systems have undoubtedly had overtime, are not reflected in the 
degree of conventional signalling which only measures the quantity of 
equipment, not their quality. The following regression proves if the 
aforementioned assumptions can be considered true.  
 
Figure 6-14: Comparison and linear regression analysis of the relationship between opening year 
and degree of conventional signalling of the analysed LR lines. The blue dots are the networks 
considered in the regression analysis; the red box refers to Copenhagen Ring 3 LR project. 
The linear regression analysis, as expected, do not shows neither 
statistically relevant results (Significance F = 0,3999 >> 0,05) nor a strong fit 
with the data (R2 = 0,1029). So, as previously stated, the quantity of signalling 
employed in the LR lines, represented by the degree of conventional signalling, is 
not related with their opening year. 
6.3.3.11 Turnout per Kilometre - Speed 
An analysis that is very interesting to do for its signalling implications, is 
the number of turnouts per kilometre compared with the average speed. In fact, 
it expected that the higher the number of turnouts, the lower the resulting 
operational speed. This is due to the functioning principles of detection devices, 
interlocking, and blade switching mechanism (see Chapter 3) that has the 
potential to slow down the LRV. 
==PAGE:139==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: INTERNATIONAL BENCHMARKING 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         116 
                 
 
 
Figure 6-15: Comparison and linear regression analysis of the relationship between the quantity of 
turnouts in the analysed lines and their average speed. The blue dots are the networks considered in 
the regression analysis; the red box refers to Copenhagen Ring 3 LR project. 
In this case, the logarithmic regression analysis do not prove the expected 
result (R2 = 0,1568). However, since the line of Dresden has a significantly higher 
amount of turnouts per kilometres, another analysis is carried out without that 
outlier. 
 
Figure 6-16: Comparison and linear regression analysis of the relationship between the quantity of 
turnouts in the analysed lines and their average speed, without the outlier (Dresden). The blue dots 
are the networks considered in the regression analysis; the red box refers to Copenhagen Ring 3 
project. 
 
Also when the outlier is removed, the result aforementioned does not 
==PAGE:140==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: INTERNATIONAL BENCHMARKING 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         117 
                 
 
change. Namely, the concentration of turnouts in the analysed lines does not 
affect their average speed. 
6.3.3.12 Maximum Allowable Speed – Average Speed 
The last analysis is the comparison between maximum allowable speed 
and average line speed. This is a very important analysis in terms of signalling. In 
fact, higher allowable speed requires a greater amount of signalling equipment 
and, more importantly, the drive-on-sight regime is usually not practicable, from 
the safety standpoint, above 70 km/h (see Chapter 0). Following the same 
principles as conventional rail, it is hypothesized that higher allowable speed 
results higher average speed (Givoni and Banister 2012). 
 
Figure 6-17: Comparison and linear regression analysis of the relationship between the maximum 
allowable speed in the analysed lines and their average speed. The blue dots are the networks 
considered in the regression analysis; the red box refers to Copenhagen Ring 3 project. 
As it was before hypothesized, there is a statistical significant relationship 
between maximum allowable speed and average speed (Significance F = 0,0322 
< 0,05) and the fit of the data is acceptable (R2 = 0,0322). So, it can be concluded 
that it is worthy to invest in the implementation of signalling system allowing 
higher operation speed. This is true in those cases where the topographical 
layout of the alignment, the urban features of the city where the LR is realized in, 
and inter-station distances make higher allowable speed feasible.  
6.3.3.13 Conclusions 
The findings of the previous analyses are summarized in the following 
table.

from 6.3.3.1 to 6.3.3.12 
The results of the analyses that have been carried out show that, from the 
statistical standpoint, the parameters that effect LR operational performances 
the most are: 
 
Station spacing 
 
Segregated right-of-way 
 
Maximum allowable speed line 
In terms of signalling, the analyses have shown that the system is 
generally effective in fulfilling the goals it is designed for, both regarding 
conventional signalling and IPMS. In fact, generally, the signalling system 
prevents unnecessary slowdown in potentially dangerous circumstances, it 
allows reaching higher speed in a safety manner, and it precludes LRV to be 
effected by road traffic at intersections. Accordingly, the results show that 
signalling systems are a valuable asset in all those circumstances where safety 
may be compromised or where higher operational performances need to be 
achieved. 
However, a more detailed analysis that considers multiple parameters, 
==PAGE:142==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
LIGHT RAIL SIGNALLING: INTERNATIONAL BENCHMARKING 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         119 
                 
 
such as actual average waiting time at LR-road intersections, before turnouts, 
before any signals, and LR operation reliability (in terms of punctuality and 
headway time variance), is suggested for a deeper understanding of signalling-
related factors that potentially affect LR operations. This was not done in the 
present thesis as the aforementioned analyses are considered to be consistent 
with the degree of detail of the present study. Thus, the comparisons done are 
appropriate with the time and space allocated for this section of the thesis, as it 
gives a first overview of the main signalling-related factors affecting LR 
operations and speed.

7 APPLICATION: THE COPENHAGEN LR RING 
3 SIGNALLING 
7.1 Introduction to the LR on Ring 3 Project 
The municipalities along Copenhagen’s Ring 3, together with the Region 
Sjaelland and the Danish state, are financing the project for the construction of a 
LR line along Ring 3. This section analysis is main aspects and expected 
performances. 
7.1.1 Purpose of the Project 
In 1947 the urban development plan for the Great Copenhagen Area, the 
so-called Finger Plan, was issued (Årkrog 2015). This plan provided for urban 
development in the suburbs around Copenhagen along five fingers, with the 
palm of the hand located in Copenhagen’s inner city.  
 
Figure 7-1: An old illustration of the Copenhagen's finger plan. Source: 
http://www.denstoredanske.dk/@api/deki/files/97747/=278157.501.jpg [24/05/2014] 
As a result, the finger plan caused in the first place transport need along 
the five fingers, but the traffic between them has also been increasing overtime. 
To satisfy those needs, as part of the original plan, five suburban railways were 
built on the fingers and one line crosses all the fingers in the immediate outskirt 
of the inner city. But, at the present moment, the transport need between the 
fingers in the outer part of the Great Copenhagen Area is fulfilled by busses only. 
==PAGE:144==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
APPLICATION: THE COPENHAGEN LR RING 3 SIGNALLING 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         121 
                 
 
Therefore, the LR line along the third ring between the fingers is imagined as a 
new and more modern mobility solution for that corridor.  
The aim of the LR on Ring 3 project is to provide better options for 
commuters as well as to built a powerful enabler for urban growth in the cities 
around Copenhagen that the LR on Ring 3 passes through. The goal is to offer 
shorter travel times and better riding comfort than the existing busses. As a 
result, the municipalities traversed by the future LR expect to attract new 
businesses and initiate new profitable urban development projects. Besides, 
from the environmental standpoint, the aim of the project is to replace pollutant 
busses with electric-driven LRV and incentivize the use of the LR by the 
commuters, instead of using their private cars (Årkrog 2015). 
7.1.2 General Line Data and Facts 
The new LR line will run across all five fingers outside Copenhagen, 
linking the various existing suburban train lines. It is planned to run between 
Lundofte (North) and Ishøj (South) in the greater Copenhagen area, the expected 
opening year 2021.  
The key information of the new LR line are (Fakta om Hovedstadens 
Letbane 2015): 
 
It will be constructed from 2016 and it is going to be opened in 2021 
 
According to the patronage forecasts, the new LR on Ring 3 will have 13-
14 million passengers per year  
 
It will stop at 6 S-train stations, 1 intercity rail station, at the Technical 
University of Denmark, and at two hospitals 
 
The total costs, including acquisition of rolling stok, amount to 5,5bn DKK 
 
The total length of the line is 27 kilometers 
 
27 stations will be constructed  
 
27 vehicles will be bought  
 
The alignment goes through eight municipalities  
 
The LR runs every 5 minutes in weekdays and every 10 minutes at night 
and on weekends 
 
The LR will have an expected maximum speed of 70 km/h; the average 
speed will be around 30 km/h 
 
4.000 passengers per day are expected to replace cars with the light rail 
for commuting  
Figure 7-2 features the overall scheme of rail-based form of transport in 
the greater Copenhagen area, with both the existing and future lines; while
