7.1.3 Regulations and Basic Project Assumptions 
According to the LR road consultant report, the regulatory framework for 
LR design in Denmark does not entirely cover every aspect of LR projects 
(COWIO3 2015b). In fact, the Danish Standards are used as basis for the general 
design of roads and junctions. However, specific design rules or project 
standards for LR do not exist in Denmark, so it has been decided to apply same 
procedures as for the other Danish LR projects. Meaning, the design is largely 
based on the German BOStrab. This regulation is closely integrated with the 
German road rules, in the LR on Ring 3 project it has been applied only in cases 
where no Danish standards exist. In addition, more specific German road 
regulations have been accounted for, like for example the signalling (RiLSA 2010 
- Richtlinien für Lichtsignalanlagen) and the road standards for pedestrian (EFA) 
and cycle traffic (ERA). The last-mentioned two road regulations govern the 
design of safe crossing with LR. Apart from the above-mentioned publications, 
inspiration has been found from experience in construction and operation of LR 
in other countries. It has to be mentioned that the application of BOStrab ensures 
to a wide extent unlimited restrictions in terms of choice of supplier of rolling 
stock. In fact, that German regulation is perceived as a customary framework 
allowing off-the-shelf solutions. 
==PAGE:148==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
APPLICATION: THE COPENHAGEN LR RING 3 SIGNALLING 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         125 
                 
 
7.2 Conceptual Design of the Signalling System 
This chapter deals with the proposed conceptual design of the signalling 
system for the Copenhagen LR on Ring 3 project. A part features the solution of 
the IPMS and the next the LR signalling. 
7.2.1 Overall Signalling System Requirements and Scheme 
According to the public sponsoring entity Metroselskabet, the LR 
signalling system should fulfil the following operational performance 
requirements (COWIO3 2015b) (RARTJV 2014a): 
 
Provide high reliability and high performance ensuring that reductions in 
line speed are kept to a minimum level throughout the signalled areas 
 
Drive-on-sight operations 
 
Maximum operating speed of 70 km/h 
 
Proposed service frequency of 5 minutes on each route (Monday to 
Saturday) 
 
With the exception of terminal stations, there is currently no requirement 
for single track bi-directional operation 
 
A high level of automation is a fundamental requirement 
 
Automation options shall be considered for the efficient working of the LR 
within the depot area 
The overall scheme of the proposed signalling system for the Copenhagen 
LR on Ring 3 project, prepared by the LR transportation system consultants, is 
depicted in Figure 7-5. The system and its components will be explained in detail 
in the following sections, the figure below is provided only to show a high level 
overview of the complete proposed LR Signalling System (RARTJV 2015a). 
==PAGE:149==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
APPLICATION: THE COPENHAGEN LR RING 3 SIGNALLING 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         126 
                 
 
 
Figure 7-5: Proposed signalling system overall architecture, prepared by the LR transportation 
system consultants. Source: RARTJV 2015a 
7.2.2 Intersection Priority Management 
In the case of LR-roads intersections with traffic light, the signalling 
system only enables the LRV to request priority. The LR transportation system 
consultants propose a simple, standard solution for all junctions and signals 
controlled by Road Traffic Signals which is outlined in the Figure 7-6 below 
(RARTJV 2015a).  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:150==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
APPLICATION: THE COPENHAGEN LR RING 3 SIGNALLING 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         127 
                 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
In all the LR-road intersections controlled by traffic light the following 
system and sequence of events is proposed (the numbers and steps refer to 
Figure 7-6):  
1. The LRV is positioned by a suitable Vehicle Location system. This is a 
standard solution made up from a number of subsystems which are:  
a. Wireless and Odometer 
b. Detector devices as a combination of physical loops, virtual loops, 
tags or track circuits (exact combination to be determined in a 
later stage)   
2. The LRV approaches the signalized junction in question and sends a signal 
at a predetermined point. It does this via a local interface directly to the 
local road traffic light signal controller to request a priority route via the 
IBIS.  
3. The opportunity to link the ITCS to the UTC is considered as an option in 
the conceptual design and it is shown for information only. This concept 
will be considered in more detail during the Tender Design Phase of the 
Copenhagen LR on Ring 3 project. 
 
Besides, the LR transportation system consultants point out that Figure 
7-6 only indicates an overall functional flow of information and does not depict 
Figure 7-6: Intersection priority request overall architecture, as proposed by the technical system 
consultants. Source: Source: RARTJV 2015a 
==PAGE:151==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
APPLICATION: THE COPENHAGEN LR RING 3 SIGNALLING 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         128 
                 
 
the exact type and extent of required signalling equipment. For example, it is 
assumed that there will be a need for some form of local interface at the Traffic 
Signal to receive and process the LRV priority request which is not shown in the 
diagram.  
The phases of the traffic lights have been designed in order to ensure a 
separate phase for the light rail (COWIO3 2015b). In most cases, this phase can 
happen simultaneously with the remaining road traffic. For example, in a 
situation where the alignment is in the centre of the road and the LRV co-runs 
with cars simultaneously. The purpose of a separate phase of the light rail is to 
ensure as much flexibility in the traffic light phases as possible. In fact, with this 
phase design, it will be possible to detect the LRV and thereby prioritise its way 
through the junctions. At the same time, at junctions where no LRVs are 
recognised, the LRV phase can be omitted and priority given to other traffic 
flows. The general aim for the signalling design is to obtain a service level for 
roads which, in most cases, corresponds to existing conditions.  
In the conceptual design effort has been made in giving priority to LRV in 
as much junctions as possible. Some junctions, however, have been designed to 
give road traffic the same or higher priority than LRV due to high road traffic 
flows. 
7.2.3 Conventional Signalling 
One of the most important parts were signalling equipment are needed are those 
containing at least one motorised turnout and a point’s position indicators, 
meaning a semaphore installed before the turnout. Turnouts will be provided at 
terminal stations and the depot entrance. Additional operationally required 
crossovers are installed in key locations that can be used in the event of 
perturbation. Turnouts may be provided with automated heating systems for 
safety and reliability during wintery conditions (RARTJV 2014a). 
To date, the only LR controlled areas envisaged are:  
 
Ishøj terminus  
 
Lundtofte terminus 
 
Glostrup 
 
Depot and maintenance centre’s entrance   
These are localised control systems, meaning that they do not have any 
connection with the control room. 
The system proposed by the transportation system consultant is set out in 
Figure 7-7 (RARTJV 2015a). 
==PAGE:152==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
APPLICATION: THE COPENHAGEN LR RING 3 SIGNALLING 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         129 
                 
 
  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
In LR only controlled areas the following system and sequence of events 
is envisaged the numbers and steps refer to Figure 7-7): 
1. The LRV is positioned by a suitable Vehicle Location system. This is a 
standard solution made up from a number of subsystems which are:  
a. Wireless and Odometer 
b. Detector devices as a combination of physical loops, virtual loops, 
tags or track circuits (exact combination to be determined in a 
later stage)   
2. As the LRV approaches the controlled area in question the vehicle will 
communicate, at a predetermined point (A), with the Point Controller 
Cabinet (PCC) to request a move of the turnout in question 
3. Once the request is received, the safety critical track blocking circuit and 
detection devices B, C’, and C” check whether the part of track 
immediately before and after the turnout are clear from other vehicles or 
not, and report that information to the PCC. 
4. If the section is free, the PCC commands to the point machine to move the 
blades to the desired position requested by the LRV. The turnout’s blades 
are now electrically locked and the turnout’s signals indicate the relevant 
Figure 7-7: Signalling solutions for the part of the line containing at least one motorized point, as proposed by 
the technical system consultants. Source: Source: RARTJV, Light Rail on Ring 3, Conceptual Design Report 
[28th February 2015] 
==PAGE:153==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
APPLICATION: THE COPENHAGEN LR RING 3 SIGNALLING 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         130 
                 
 
point position. The LRV can now proceed and traverse the turnout with 
the pre-set discretion. Once the vehicle enters the blocking circuit, the 
point controller remains locked until the vehicle exits the blocking circuit 
with delocking performed by the detection devices operated in the 
correct sequence. 
7.3 Actual and Potential Signalized Zones 
The present chapter explains in detail all those particular zones and 
circumstances where signalling equipment are designed to be installed and other 
where their installation may bring some advantages.  
7.3.1 Glostrup Station 
The proposed track layout for Glostrup (Figure 7-8), an S-train 
interchange station, is really peculiar and presents a number of challenges, both 
operationally and from the signalling standpoint.  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Particularly, the following difficulties are found (RARTJV 2014b): 
 
Conflict zone with bi-directional single track operations, despite that 3 or 
4 tracks will be built 
 
Track 3 is not used in regular operations 
 
The LRV has to reverse driving direction and the driver has to change cab, 
with 2 minutes of scheduled dwell time 
 
If some switches fail, the operations between Ishøj and Lundofte are not 
guaranteed    
Figure 7-8: Proposed layout for Glostrup station. On the left, the indication of the conflict zone and 
the level crossing. On the right, the expected LRV routes. Source: RARTJV 2014b 
==PAGE:154==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
APPLICATION: THE COPENHAGEN LR RING 3 SIGNALLING 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         131 
                 
 
 
Each train crosses the road two times, one to get in and the other to get 
out from Glostrup 
 
In peak hours, the LRV crosses the road 4 times in 5 minutes 
 
Glostrup would be like a third terminal in the line, representing an unique 
case in the other to benchmarked lines. 
Accordingly, conventional signalling is needed in the conflict zone. 
Besides, the potential for delays is considered high, both for LRV and cars, due to 
the LR-road intersections and other operational conditions.

By analysing the proposed alternatives, it seems that in order to complete 
solve the issues in Glostrup (LR conflict and double crossing of roads) a 
combination of at least two proposals should be implemented. In fact, no one of 
the proposal alone has the potential for effectively solving both issues. 
7.3.1.2 People Mover Alternative 
Another alternative that has not been considered could be to replace the 
portion of LR alignment going from the Ring 3 road to Glostrup station with a 
cable-propelled people mover. This solution is successfully implemented in 
several other cities (Neumann 1998) and there is potential for off-the-shelf 
solutions.  
 
Table 7-2: Example of a people mover, that could replace the LR alignment at Glostrup station. 
Source: 
https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/People_mover#/media/File:Birmingham_airport_rapid_transit_syste
m.JPG [12th June 2015] 
A people mover that can potentially replace the part of the LR alignment 
has the following data (transport data from Simeone and Papa 2010): 
 
Line Length = 450 meters 
 
Bottom-supported single track with central loop 
 
No intermediate stations 
 
To be realized on the space designed for the LR alignment 
 
Underpass under Ring 3 
 
Max speed = 7-9 m/s 
 
Travel Time = between 1’ 10” and 1’ 30” 
 
Number of vehicles = 2 
 
Vehicle capacity = 40 – 200 people per vehicle 
 
Dwell time = 30 seconds 
 
Minimum frequency = circa 3’ 30” 
If this solution is implemented, the advantage for the LR could be: 
==PAGE:156==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
APPLICATION: THE COPENHAGEN LR RING 3 SIGNALLING 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         133 
                 
 
 
No need for conventional signalling and switches in Glostrup 
 
LR conflicts are solved and there are not bidirectional single track 
operations 
 
Ease start up and ramp down operations from/to the CMC 
 
There won’t be any LR-road intersections at Glostrup 
 
Robust solution 
 
The risk of service interruption in Glostrup is cancelled 
 
Significantly shorter travel time (there could be potential for buying one

 
Off-the-shelf solution 


On the other hand, the main disadvantages are: 
 
Potentially higher cost (to be determined) 
 
Additional transport modal shift for passenger  
 
Different design and supplier than LR transport system 
 
It may not be ready on time when LR opens in 2021 (initially, there could 
be bus replacement services). 
Of course, this is a first preliminary input for a possible solution that has 
the potential of solve the signalling and operational issues related with the 
layout of Glostrup station. A more detailed analysis, which would go beyond the 
scope of the present thesis, is however suggested.  
7.3.2 Terminals 
Both terminals in Ishøj and in Lundofte are designed with a crossover 
before the stop. All the 4 turnouts will be mechanically thrown remotely. This is 
a standard solution and consistent with what explained in Chapter 4.1.1.2.  
 
Figure 7-9: Plan design of the two line’s terminals. On the left Ishøj, and Lundofte on the right. 
(COWIO3 2015b) 
==PAGE:157==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
APPLICATION: THE COPENHAGEN LR RING 3 SIGNALLING 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         134 
                 
 
However, potentially 3 of the 4 switches could be trailable sprung 
turnouts with the blades set in the following position, allowing normal 
operations: 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
So, the only mechanized turnout actualizes the driver’s command 
regarding the platform that is going to be used by the LRV in the terminal. In case 
of contingency of operation (e.g. need for left hand operations) the sprung 
turnouts can be moved by means of a manual local system. This solution would 
minimize costs in terms of signalling, turnout’s acquisition, and maintenance. On 
the other hand, it reduces flexibility in case of contingency and limits the 
potential for remote control of the turnouts. 
Another option could be to place a single sprung crossover after the 
terminal (see Chapter 4.1.1.3). This solution would be cheaper as there is need 
for three turnouts less and there is no need for interlocking. But it reduces 
redundancy at the terminal; if the switch fails, there are not operational 
alternative for reverse LRV direction. 
7.3.3 Control and Maintenance Centre 
The purpose of the CMC is to provide an efficient and wide enough area 
for the maintenance and storage of all LRV, as well as a central point for the 
driving staff. The CMC is to meet operational and technical requirements, 
including employment of staff, operation of vehicles and execution of the 
maintenance, such as safe parking of the LRT vehicles, execution of preparatory 
and terminating work as well as administration of staff and vehicles (RARTJV 
2014a). The design of the CMC will respond to the following statements: 
 
Optimisation of working time thanks to a minimal time wasting for the 
vehicles moving on site 
 
Optimisation and segregation of all movements to avoid accidents 
TERMINAL 
MACHINE 
SPRING 
SPRING 
SPRING 
Figure 7-10: Alternative for the terminal turnouts: 3 of the 4 switching mechanism are replaced with 
tailable sprung points. 
==PAGE:158==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
APPLICATION: THE COPENHAGEN LR RING 3 SIGNALLING 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         135 
                 
 
 
Optimisation of the global maintenance and location of the facilities and 
equipment 
Those requirements result in the safety-by-design principles presented in 
Chapter 4.2. The aim is to keep signalling equipment to a minimum level and at 
the same time avoiding potentially dangerous situations, such as bidirectional 
single track operations. However, a dedicated signalling and CMC management 
systems at will have to be installed. As a result, an anti-clockwise track layout 
has been designed to meet those requirements. The entrance to the CMC will be 
trough remotely controlled motorized turnouts (RARTJV 2015a). 
 
Figure 7-11: Site plan of the CMC, as presented by RARTJV in the Conceptual Design. Source: RARTJV 
2015a. 
Besides, the signalling system will be supervised from the CMC Operating 
Room at the LR depot. The signalling supervisory system will be presented to the 
operator by using a Graphical User Interface, combining all information 
necessary to control the system safely. The AVL, detection devices, the IBIS, the 
SCADA, and the ITCS, are all systems that are employed to get information about 
the status of the LRV as well as the components of the signalling system (RARTJV 
2014). 
7.3.4 Lyngby Station and Underpass 
A location where the signalling system is not expected to be installed, but 
it might be needed, is in the tunnel underneath the S-train line in Lyngby. The 
==PAGE:159==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
APPLICATION: THE COPENHAGEN LR RING 3 SIGNALLING 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         136 
                 
 
location features a 90 meters long LR-only tunnel, followed by a very tight curve 
(radius of 25 meters) with lateral obstructions; making the expected visibility 
low. Besides, the alignment in that area has a 6% gradient, making the braking 
distance of southbound trains significantly longer than on a flat surface. 
 
Figure 7-12: Plan of the designed LR alignment in the area where signalling system may be installed. 
Source: COWIO3 2015b 
In this area, the set LR maximum allowable speed is as low as 15 km/h 
and there is a station immediately after the curve, so it is expected that the LRV 
will be actually travelling with low speed. However, the installation of a 
signalling system with track occupancy detection devices, a simple interlocking, 
and a signal per direction before the curve and tunnel, is expected to increase 
operational safety. Conversely, the risk of rear-end collisions increases in the 
case that a LRV stops in the tunnel and the driver of the following LRV has 
troubles seeing it.  
As a result, the installation of a simple signalling system, with detection 
starting immediately before and finishing immediately after the tunnel and 
curve, would significantly decrease the crashes likelihood. However, it has to be 
mentioned that due to the low radius, the operation speed is not expected to be 
increased by the installation of such system. 
7.3.5 Final Comment on Signalling 
The signalling design for the Copenhagen LR on Ring 3 project seems to 
be overall consistent with the LR signalling system and IPMS goals and 
functionalities presented in the previous chapters. However, some alternatives 
that may improve operation performances and safety could be further studied, 
such as the issue of Glostrup station and the underpass in Lyngby station. 
==PAGE:160==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
APPLICATION: THE COPENHAGEN LR RING 3 SIGNALLING 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         137 
                 
 
7.4 Benchmarking  
The aim of the Benchmarking is to check where the Copenhagen LR on Ring 3 
project stands, compared with other LR lines from all around the World. By 
doing that, a performance assessment is carried out and some potential project 
guidelines and inputs may be found.  
First, the line data are calculated and presented, next they are 
benchmarked and analysed, and finally some suggestions for improvements are 
shown.  
7.4.1 Line Data 
The following table holds a number of relevant quantitative data for the 
Copenhagen LR on Ring 3. Those are the data that have been used in the 
previous Section 6 to benchmark the LR on Ring 3 against other LR networks. 
The table and the relevance of the parameters it held are explained in detail in 
the Chapter 6.3.1 on page 93. 
Since the line object of study is, at the present time, on a design phase, it 
has to be mentioned that some alignment alternatives are under assessment and 
some LR parameters, such as travel time, may eventually vary slightly. However, 
it is expected that the variations will no significantly change the results. The data 
presented in the following table are relative to the alignment and parameters as 
presented in the Conceptual Design Reports from the consultants (COWI03

7.4.2 Benchmark of Copenhagen LR on Ring 3 Project 
The most relevant information for the Ring 3 LR project, resulting from 
the comparison, are: 
 
Its average speed stands in the medium-high part of the other line’s data 
range. This is consistent with the topography and the population density 
==PAGE:162==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
APPLICATION: THE COPENHAGEN LR RING 3 SIGNALLING 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         139 
                 
 
of the areas T LR goes through 
 
The average distance between stations is slightly higher than the majority 
of the other analysed lines; considering the results from the analysis (see 
Chapter 6.3.3.13), this factor is likely to be the one leading to higher Ring 
3’s average operational speed 
 
The Ring 3 stations distribution inequality (represented by the Gini 
coefficient) is within the range between 15% and 30 %, where the 
majority of the lines stand 
 
The average distance between intersections with traffic light is the 
highest among the analyses lines and twice as high as the mean of the 
average distance between intersections (mean = 0,23km; Ring 3  
= 0,47 km). However, this data is not expected to affect the average LR 
speed, as proved in the analysis in Chapter 6.3.3.4 
 
The Ring 3 intersections distribution inequality (represented by the Gini 
coefficient) is within the range between 25% and 50 %, where the 
majority of the lines stand 
 
Regarding the IPMS architecture, in the majority of the other lines the 
request is either sent directly to the road traffic controller or is sent via 
UTC, with the direct request as a fall-back system; in this regard, the 
project for the Ring 3 does not show any anomaly in term of architecture 
 
The LR on Ring 3 is able to achieve significantly higher speed than lines 
having comparable right-of-way composition. Or, it has circa the same 
speed as the line of Amsterdam that is built on a fully exclusive alignment 
(but the average distance between station is also lower in the Dutch case). 
 
The conventional signalling needed, for safety purposes, in Copenhagen is 
limited. In fact, the degree of conventional signalling is in the low part of 
the variation range 
 
The headway time is the second-lowest among the analysed lines, 
representing a good parameter in terms of potential passenger 
expectation 
 
The amount of turnouts is low, compared with the other cities. This might 
be due to the lack of connections with other LR lines in the same network. 
 
Among the lines with 70km/h as maximum allowable speed with drive-
on-sight running regime, Copenhagen is the second-best performing line.  
As a result, the Copenhagen LR Ring 3 project is expected to have overall 
quantitative performances within the variation range of the other analysed lines, 
with the exception of the average distance between intersections. Besides, it is 
==PAGE:163==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
APPLICATION: THE COPENHAGEN LR RING 3 SIGNALLING 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         140 
                 
 
expected that the overall signalling system design will not present extraordinary 
challenges. In fact, the circumstances where conventional signalling is needed 
are limited, as represented by the degree of conventional signalling and 
according to what is described in Chapter 7.3. Also, as the number of intersection 
with traffic light per kilometre is significantly lower than the average, the IPMS 
definition process is expected to be not particularly challenging, at least from the 
technical standpoint. In fact, intersections that are placed at a greater distance 
between each other potentially require less coordination efforts. Moreover, since 
the alignment is, to a greater extend, semi-exclusive, LR operations should be 
less affected by unpredictable road traffic. 
7.4.3 Proposals for Improvement with Inputs from Benchmarking 
After the benchmarking of the Copenhagen LR on Ring 3 project has been 
carried out, at least two possible areas of improvements has been found. One 
regards a potential increase in average speed and another one a different 
architecture for the intersection priority management system. 
7.4.3.1 Increasing Maximum Allowable Speed  
As a suggestion, it might be considered whether is practicable to increase 
the maximum allowable speed to 80 km/h, at least in the less populated areas of 
the alignment, leading to shorter travel times. This solution would impact on the 
amount of signalling equipment that needs to be installed and the drive-on-sight 
regime that could not be implemented. However, it would lead to savings in 
terms of travel time and, possibly, in terms of rolling stock acquisition and 
maintenance. 
This suggestion is formulated after the benchmarking considering that 
there are a number of parameters that may successfully allow higher maximum 
speed: 
 
The average station spacing is high (1,0 km) with 11 inter-station 
distances higher than 1,0 km and the longest is as high as 2,49 km; giving 
enough length for the LRV to speed up to the maximum speed. 
 
The average intersection distance is higher than the average, thus the 
number of dangerous point is lower. 
 
The degree of conventional signalling is low; installing more signalling 
equipment should not lead to excessively big and hard to manage 
signalling system 
 
The headway time of 5 minutes does not conflict with the functionalities 
of the most common signalling systems that need to be installed 
==PAGE:164==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
APPLICATION: THE COPENHAGEN LR RING 3 SIGNALLING 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         141 
                 
 
 
Three, among nine of the analysed lines, allow higher operational speed 
than 70 km/h; therefore, it is assumed that there are off-the-shelf 
solutions available on the market that supports higher speed. 
Accordingly, it seems that from the pure technical stand point increasing 
the maximum speed is feasible and would lead to increased performances. 
However, it requires a greater investment in signalling equipment that safely 
supports higher speed, since the drive-on-sight is not allowed. Also, in order to 
further increase safety, the level of separation of the LR right-of-way from roads 
may need to be upgraded with higher curbs of fences. Besides, from the legal 
standpoint, there might be some constraints that inhibit LRV to travel faster. For 
instance, the German BOStrab set the maximum allowable speed to 70km/h; 
however, this is not a binding regulation in Denmark and other guidelines and 
standards can be followed.  
As a conclusion, raising the maximum allowable speed is expected to lead 
to increased operational performances that need to be quantified. Besides, this 
decision needs to be taken within a legal framework that allows that. 
Accordingly, it is suggested to further investigate and analyse this topic to a 
higher degree of detail than the one consistent with the present thesis.  
7.4.3.2 Different Architecture of the Intersection Priority Management System  
After carefully benchmark the Copenhagen LR against the other lines, as 
well as analysing the pros and cons of the technical solutions available, a 
proposal for possible improvement of Copenhagen LR’s IPMS is formulated. 
Namely, the creation of a single LR-UTC for the whole Ring 3 that regulates the 
road traffic in the zones surrounding the LR alignment and deals with LR priority 
request. This unique LR-UTC would cover the entire LR alignment, despite the 
different municipal UTC that may be implemented in the future. The basic 
functionality of this system are: 
 
The IBIS send the priority request to the ITCS, that forward it to the LR-
UTC 
 
The LR-UTC manage the LR priority request and realize a macro-
regulation of the road traffic in the road that surround the LR alignment 
 
The importance of various traffic flows, LR included, can vary dynamically 
during the day, according to various parameters such as traffic status, 
planned events, etc. However, it is suggested to set higher LR priority to 
achieve higher operational performances 
 
Direct priority request as a fall-back system with radio communication 
between the LRV and the single road traffic controller 
==PAGE:165==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
APPLICATION: THE COPENHAGEN LR RING 3 SIGNALLING 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         142 
                 
 
 
If single municipalities decide, in the future, to install their own UTC, it 
can be interfaced with the LR-UTC and manage traffic in roads that are 
out of the area controlled by the LR-UTC 
 
The detection of LRV can be done as the consultant proposes, aided with 
GNSS  
The main advantages of this solution, compared with the proposed one, are: 
 
A live macro-regulation of the traffic in the areas surrounding the LR 
alignment can be done  
 
The LR-UTC could be managed directly by the LR operator that sets the 
priority level accordingly, in collaboration with other stakeholders and 
under the surveillance of relevant traffic authorities  
 
Priority request from other relevant vehicles (emergency vehicles, busses, 
etc.) can also be managed by the LR-UTC 
 
Road traffic can be managed in the roads next to the alignment and, 
accordingly, there are more chances to free intersection that are blocked 
by road vehicle due to heavy traffic before the LRV arrives at the crossing  
 
The implementation of the LR-UTC can be done directly by the LR project 
office  
 
There can be an interface and a live exchange of traffic information 
between the LR-UTC and the municipal UTCs, if they will be implemented, 
allowing an harmonic traffic regulation on a large scale 
 
The communication reliability and the redundancy level between LRV and 
road traffic controller is high, due to double communication channels (via 
ITCS-UTC and direct) 
On the other hand, the disadvantages than the solution proposed by the 
consultants are: 
 
Higher implementation costs, since an extra system needs to be 
implemented 
 
The municipalities may disagree with the implementation on their 
territory of a system they cannot directly manage 
Overall, this is a more expensive solution that has the potential for 
making LR operations slightly faster and more reliable (there is potential for 
saving in the operation phase) as well as making road traffic more fluid. This 
system has been implemented in the Line 1 of Florence LR. Thanks to the success 
proven by 5 years of operations, has been decided to implement it in the other 
five lines under construction or in the design phase. 
==PAGE:166==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         143 
                 
 
7.4.4 Tentative for a Qualitative Benchmarking 
The aim of a qualitative benchmarking regarding the signalling systems 
and IPMS is to get specific and in-depth qualitative information of the solutions 
implemented in other networks. Once this information are get, the solutions 
chosen for the Copenhagen LR on Ring 3 project can be thoroughly compared 
with other lines’ solution. This would give diverse and additional project 
indication and guide compared with the information get with the quantitative 
benchmarking presented in the previous chapter. 
Therefore, a questionnaire regarding signalling and IPMS solution 
(presented in Appendix B on page 168) has been issued to relevant people 
appointed of the signalling system in the following cities: 
 
Florence 
 
Lyon 
 
Minneapolis/Saint Paul 
 
Mannheim 
 
UK cities: Blackpool/Manchester/Nottingham/Edinburgh 
Unfortunately, overall the responses have not been numerous and 
detailed enough to provide relevant and consistent benchmarking guidelines. In 
fact, the only questionnaire thoroughly answered has been from Lyon, partial 
responses came from Mannheim and from the British cities, and no replies came 
from Florence and Minnesota. Therefore, the tentative for a qualitative 
benchmarking has not been successful.  
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:167==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
CONCLUSIONS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         144 
                 
 
CONCLUSIONS 
The aim of creating an all-embracing document dealing with LR signalling 
has been achieved. The thesis features a systematic holistic approach in studying 
LR signalling system and its components. A significant part of it deals with an 
analysis of the system working principles and philosophies, the following 
carefully examine the system equipment and components. Next, it has been 
proven how the working philosophies are reflected both in the functionalities of 
the single components and in the signalling system as a whole. 
The benchmarking part of the thesis required a significant amount of time 
and effort. Both because the relative literature is not extensive, and data are 
difficult to obtain. However, the comparison of key LR operational and signalling 
data gave interesting outcomes. In fact, the analyses proved the expected results, 
reflecting the LR signalling system functionalities and philosophies previously 
presented. Besides, the comparison between various indicators showed how the 
signalling system does not interfere negatively with LR performance, Moreover, 
it has also been proven that the IPMS is effective in not slowing down LRV at 
intersections.  
The gathered knowledge and outcomes from the benchmarking have 
been applied for carrying out an analysis of the proposed signalling solutions for 
the Copenhagen LR on Ring 3 Project, considering its unique peculiarities. The 
overall assessment of the project for the LR signalling and IPMS reflect the 
working principles presented in the first part of the thesis. Besides, when 
benchmarked with the lines, the LR on Ring 3 should achieve comparable 
performances. However, a number of potentially problematic situations have 
been found, especially regarding the layout in Glostrup Station. In this regard, 
suggestions for improvement have been formulated, taking into account both the 
knowledge gathered in the first chapter and the results from the benchmarking. 
They may represent precious project guidance and further inspiration for the 
design team, both of LR Ring 3 and other lines. 
As further studies indication, it is suggested to carry out a qualitative 
benchmarking analysis, to compare and contrast the results from the quantitate 
analysis. Besides, in order to gather an even stronger insight for the signalling-
related factors affecting LR safety and operation, it is suggested to study a 
number of additional parameters. For example, analyses of the waiting time of 
LRV at LR-intersections as well as before turnouts and any signal are expected to 
provide a deeper understanding of the signalling-related parameters that affect 
LR operations. Finally, it would be recommendable to carry out an analysis on 
==PAGE:168==
M.Sc. Thesis in Transport and Logistics: Signalling Systems for Light Rail 
CONCLUSIONS 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         145 
                 
 
how drive-on-sight and the running regime with conventional signalling affect 
LR and road user safety in terms of crashes and fatalities, as there seems to be a 
lack in the literature covering this topic in detail.

APPENDIX B: BENCHMARKING QUESTIONNARIES 
 
By Ranieri Brandani                                                                                                                                         168 
                 
 
APPENDIX B: BENCHMARKING 
QUESTIONNARIES 
1. Which types of signalling systems are in place in your line? Which is 
the safety level implemented (Drive-on-sight or signalled sections and 
where - tunnels, single tracks, terminuses, intermediate terminuses, 
higher speed sections)? 
2. If it is drive-on-sight can you please describe the intersection priority 
management architecture and its basic functionality? How the priority 
request is formulated, send to the local road traffic controller (directly 
from the vehicle or via UTC?), accepted, cancelled, fall back 
scenario(s), and so on. 
3. In this case which is the technology used for the signalling system 
especially 
for 
the 
communication 
vehicle/traffic 
signal 
(virtual/physical loops, radio, …)? 
4. Which level of LRT priority has been implemented? (Full, high, 
medium, low at which all/some junctions)? Short description of what 
is meant for the priority level e.g. Priority given only to LR vehicles 
fulfilling pre-set running parameters (delayed, shorter headway, etc.) 
or to all LRT vehicles running in normal conditions? 
5. And how is the priority implemented? 
 at which point and how the LR phase is insert into the traffic light’s 
phases rotation (extension, recall, stake skipping, etc.)?  
 “Train ready to start system” when junction is right after a station? 
 Other? 
6. In case of failure of the signalling system, which are the fall back 
scenarios/solutions/procedures to assure LRT gets priority? 
7. Information available waiting time of LR vehicles at intersection. 
 Average waiting time at the intersections: 
 Maximum planned waiting time at the intersection: 
 In the case that the intersection priority management system was 
not installed, do you have any idea of how longer the travelling 
time would have been?
